Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
Invalidation of Qiyas: The Appropriate Difference (Farq Munasib)
Explains invalidation of qiyas by appropriate difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a qiyas is invalidated when an appropriate difference (farq munasib) exists between the original and the branch, breaking the strong presumption of causality.

Core Idea
An appropriate difference destroys the presumption that the original descriptor is the cause.

The previous lesson established when a qiyas is valid. This lesson examines one crucial factor that can invalidate it: the existence of an appropriate difference (farq munasib) between the original (asl) and the branch (far'). The passage explains that if a suitable descriptor appears in another case to which the ruling could be attributed, the original presumption of causality is broken, and the qiyas fails. This lesson uses the analogy of witness testimony to rank descriptors and shows how farq munasib functions as a rebuttal tool in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Recall the Condition for Strong Presumption
Review that a descriptor suitable and linked to consensus creates strong presumption attributing the ruling to that descriptor.
This presumption is the basis for the validity of the qiyas.
2
Define Farq Munasib
Understand 'farq munasib' as an appropriate difference between original and branch that offers another suitable descriptor.
The text says: 'بالفرق بين الأصل والفرع في الوصف المناسب' – a difference in the appropriate descriptor.
3
Explain the Invalidation Mechanism
The existence of another suitable descriptor in a different case means the original descriptor is not uniquely effective, so the presumption dissipates.
The ruling cannot be attributed with strong zann when an alternative cause exists.
4
Work Through the Witness Analogy
The passage classifies descriptors into three types (explicit text, consensus, mere suitability) analogous to witness testimony: after tazkiya, before tazkiya, mastur.
A descriptor that is textually indicated or linked to consensus is like a credible witness; one based on mere suitability is like an unknown witness.
5
Apply the Rule
Practice: given a qiyas, identify if a farq munasib exists by checking if there is another suitable descriptor in a comparable case.
If yes, the qiyas is invalid; if no, the qiyas remains valid.

Source Text

البحث الرابع في القياس فصل في القياس الشرعي وقد اقترن به الحكم في موضع الإجماع يضاف الحكمُ إليه للمناسبة لا لشهادة الشرع هذا الوصف من الأكل واللبس بكونه علةً. ونظيره: إذا رأينا شخصاً أعطى فقيراً درهماً غلب على الظن أن الإعطاء لدفع حاجة الفقير، وتحصيل مصالح الثواب. إذا عُرف هذا فنقول: إذا رأينا وصفاً مناسباً للحكم، وقد اقترن به الحكم في موضع الإجماع يغلب الظن بإضافة الحكم إلى ذلك الوصف. وغلبة الظن في الشرع توجبُ العَمَل عند انعدام ما فوقها من الدليل بمنزلة المسافر إذا غلب على ظنه أن بقربه ماءً لم يجز له التيمم، وعلى هذا مسائل التحري. وحكمُ هذا القياس أن يبطل بالفرق المناسب؛ لأن عنده يُوجَدُ مناسب سواه في صورة يضاف الحكم إليه: جواب إذا أي: إذا وجدنا مناسباً للحكم وقد اقترن به الحكم في موضع آخر من نص أو إجماع يضاف الحكم إلى ذلك الوصف كذا في "معدن الأصول". للمناسبة: لمناسبة ذلك الوصف الحكم بكونه علة: أي بكون الوصف علة كما إذا عللنا في ولاية الإنكاح في الصغير بعلة الصغر للمناسبة؛ لأن ولاية الإنكاح لم تشرع إلا على وجه النظر للصغير باعتبار عجزه عن مباشرة النكاح مع حاجة إلى مقصوده، والصغير مورث للعجز، فكان هذا تعليلاً بوصف ملائم للحكم، وقد ظهر أثر هذا الوصف في موضع الإجماع وهو ولاية المال، فإنها ثابتة في مال الصغير بالإجماع، وإنما يشترط هذا الوجوب العمل بالوصف؛ لأن الوصف في القياس بمنزلة الشاهد العدالة من وهو اجتنابه عن محظورات دينه، واجتنابه عنها يدل ظاهراً على أنه يجتنب عن الكذب في الشهادة أيضاً، فظهور أثر الوصف في موضع آخر يدل ظاهراً على أنه مؤثر في موضع النزاع، فهذا يوجب العمل بالقياس بهذه العلة، وأما مجرد المناسبة فيُحَوِّزُهُ ولا يوجبه كذا في "الفصول" و "المعدن". لم يجز له التيمُّمُ: لأن غلبة الظن عند انعدام ما فوقها من الدليل بمنزلة المتحقق. مسائل التحري كما إذا اشتبهت عليه القبلة وتحرى ووقع تحريه على شيء لغلبة الظن وليس عنده من يسأله فيجب العمل به بالفرق المناسب : أي بالفرق بين الأصل والفرع في الوصف المناسب؛ لأن عنده وجود الفرق = الحكم، فلا يبقى الظنُّ بإضافة الحكم إليه، فلا يثبتُ الحكم به؛ لأنه كان بناء على غلبة الظن، وقد بَطَل ذلك بالفرق. غلبة الظن الوصف الحكم وعلى هذا كان العَمَلَ بالنوع الأوّل بمنزلة الحكم بالشهادة بعد تزكية الشاهد وتعديله، والنوع الثاني بمنزلة الشهادة عند ظهور العدالة قبل التزكية، والنوع الثالث بمنزلة شهادة المستور. ...
The fourth inquiry on analogy. Chapter on legal analogy. And the ruling was linked to it in a place of consensus: the ruling is attributed to the descriptor for suitability, not because the law indicates this descriptor. Its example: eating and clothing as a cause. Its parallel: if we see a person giving a dirham to a poor person, it is strongly presumed that the giving is to fulfill the poor person's need and to obtain the benefits of reward. When this is known, we say: if we see a descriptor suitable for the ruling, and it was linked to the ruling in a place of consensus, strong presumption arises that the ruling is attributed to that descriptor. Strong presumption in law necessitates action when there is no evidence above it, like a traveller who strongly presumes that there is water near him – he is not permitted to perform tayammum, and similarly the cases of ijtihad (taharrī). The ruling of this analogy is that it is invalidated by an appropriate difference, because with it there exists another suitable descriptor in a different case that could be attributed the ruling. Response to 'if': i.e., if we find a descriptor suitable for the ruling and it was linked to the ruling in another place from text or consensus, the ruling is attributed to that descriptor, as in Ma'dan al-Usul. 'For suitability': because of the suitability of that descriptor to the ruling as being a cause, i.e., the descriptor being a cause – as when we give the cause for guardianship in marriage for a minor as the suitability of minority, since guardianship in marriage was legislated only for the benefit of the minor due to his inability to perform marriage despite his need for its purpose, and minority causes inability, so this is an explanation with a descriptor suitable for the ruling. And the effect of this descriptor appeared in a place of consensus, which is guardianship over property, as it is established for the minor's property by consensus. This obligation to act by the descriptor is only required because the descriptor in analogy is like the witness's 'adāla (integrity) from avoiding religious prohibitions, and his avoidance of them ostensibly indicates that he also avoids lying in testimony. So the appearance of the descriptor's effect in another place ostensibly indicates that it is effective in the disputed case, so this necessitates acting by this analogy with this cause. As for mere suitability, it makes it possible but does not necessitate it, as in al-Fusūl and al-Ma'dan. 'He is not permitted tayammum': because strong presumption when there is no evidence above it is like certainty. Cases of taharrī: like when the direction of the qibla is unclear for him, he performs taharrī and his taharrī leads to something by strong presumption, and there is no one to ask, so he must act by it. 'By an appropriate difference': i.e., by the difference between the original and the branch in the appropriate descriptor, because with him the existence of a difference = the ruling, so the presumption of attributing the ruling to the original descriptor does not remain, so the ruling is not established by it, because it was based on strong presumption, and that was nullified by the difference. Strong presumption, the descriptor, the ruling. And upon this, action with the first type is like ruling by testimony after the witness's tazkiya and ta'dīl; the second type is like testimony when integrity appears before tazkiya; the third type is like the testimony of a person of unknown character (mastūr). ...
Source pages p. 220

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the conditions of valid qiyas (analogy) in Hanafi usul. It explains that when a descriptor (wasf) is suitable (munasib) for a ruling and is linked to that ruling in a consensus case, this creates a strong presumption (ghalabat al-zann) that the descriptor is the cause ('illa). This presumption obligates action in the absence of stronger evidence, similar to the case of a traveler who presumes water is near – he cannot perform tayammum. The passage then states that such an analogy can be invalidated by a 'farq munasib' – showing an appropriate difference between the original and branch that provides another suitable descriptor. The author analogizes the descriptor's evidentiary value to witness testimony: a descriptor explicitly stated in text is like a witness after tazkiya (verified integrity); one known by consensus is like a witness whose integrity is apparent; one based solely on suitability is like a witness of unknown character (mastūr). The discussion includes examples from Hanafi fiqh, such as guardianship in marriage for minors based on minority, and the traveler analogy.

Key Terms
الفرق المناسب (al-farq al-munāsib): An appropriate difference between original and branch that provides another suitable descriptor, invalidating the original qiyas
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Strong presumption that the descriptor is the cause, arising from its appropriateness and linkage in consensus

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of a farq munasib on a qiyas?
A) It strengthens the qiyas by providing additional support.
B) It invalidates the qiyas because the strong presumption of causality is broken.
C) It has no effect unless the farq is textually proven.
D) It converts the qiyas from valid to permissible.

Key Takeaway
A qiyas is invalid when an appropriate difference (farq munasib) provides another suitable descriptor, breaking the strong presumption that the original descriptor is the cause.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

The Eight Objections to Qiyas and Classification of Causes
Lists and explains the eight standard objections in qiyas debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight standard objections to qiyas in Hanafi usul and classify descriptors (causes) based on their epistemological status as established by text, consensus, or suitability.

Core Idea
The eight objections systematically test analogical reasoning, mirroring the scrutiny of witness testimony in Islamic courts.

The eight objections (as'ila) are the standard toolkit for critiquing a qiyas argument in Hanafi usul. They are not arbitrary; they correspond to the way a litigant may challenge a claim in court. This lesson introduces each objection and then explores how descriptors (awṣāf) used as causes are classified by their mode of proof: by text, consensus, or mere suitability. Understanding this taxonomy is essential to mastering the mechanics of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the eight objections
This passage lists eight objections (as'ila) that can be raised against a qiyas. Commit them to memory: (1) al-mumāna'a, (2) al-qawl bi-mūjib al-'illa, (3) al-qalb, (4) al-'aks, (5) fasād al-wad', (6) … (the remaining three are not fully listed; the passage breaks off). In standard Hanafi works, the full list includes: 6. al-farq (distinguishing the branch), 7. al-kasr (breaking the cause), 8. al-man' (prevention). However, the text here gives only five explicitly plus an example. Focus on the first five: mumāna'a, qawl bi-mūjib, qalb, 'aks, and fasād al-wad'.
The passage is incomplete; the standard eight are documented in other sources like al-Mukhtaṣar al-Wiqāyah.
2
Understand the example of al-Shafi'i's qiyas on zakat
The passage gives an example of al-Shafi'i's argument that zakat is obligatory on a minor's property by analogy to an adult, with the commonality (jāmi') being 'fulfilling the need of the poor'. The objection (fasād al-wad' or farq) is that the cause in the original is to purify sins, which is absent in the minor. This illustrates how a difference in the illa invalidates the analogy.
This is a classic example of a legitimate ikhtilaf: Shafi'i uses a broader illa (need), while the Hanafi responder uses a more specific illa (purification).
3
Learn the three types of descriptors (causes) by epistemological status
The passage distinguishes three ways a descriptor can be known as a cause: (a) by explicit text (Quran/Hadith) – like a witness after verified tazkiya, unassailable; (b) by consensus – like a witness whose integrity is known without formal tazkiya; (c) by mere suitability (munāsaba) – like a witness of unknown character (mastur). Each type has different strength against objections.
This classification determines how easily a qiyas can be overturned.
4
Relate each descriptor type to the witness testimony analogy
The text uses the analogy of a qadi's decision based on witness testimony. A cause from text is like a fully verified witness; consensus is like a witness known to be upright; suitability is like a witness whose character is unknown. Each can be challenged differently. For instance, the first type cannot be invalidated (lā yataṣawwaru fīhi al-naqḍ).
This analogy helps in memorizing the hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأسئلة المتوجهة على القياس الأَسْوَلَةُ المتوجهة على القياس ثمانية : ١ - الممانعةُ ٢- والقول بموجب العلة ٣- والقلبُ ٤ - والعكس ٥- وفساد الوَضع = يوجد مناسب في المقيس عليه سوى الوصف الذي عللتموه، ومثال ذلك كما قال الشافعي له: يجب الزكاة في مال الصبي قياساً على البالغ، والجامع: دفع حاجة الفقير والسائل أن يبطله بالفرق المناسب وهو أن يقول: إن وجوب الزكاة في صورة موضع الإجماع لتطهير الآثام والذنوب، وهذا المعنى مفقود في صورة الفرع فلا يجب كذا في "الفصول" و"المعدن". كان العَمَلُ: أي على ما ذكرنا من الأقسام الثلاثة، والفرق بينها: أن الوصف المعلوم بالكتاب والسنة بمنزلة تزكية الشاهد المعدل من المزكي؛ لأن دلالة النص على كونه علة بمنزلة تزكية الشاهد من المزكي، والوصف المعلوم بالإجماع بمنزلة الشاهد الذي ظهر عدالته قبل التزكية؛ لأن الإجماع لا يدل صريحاً ولا إشارة على أن هذا الوصف علة. بالنوع الأوّل: هو التعليل المنصوص بالقرآن والحديث بمنزلة القضاء بشهادة الشهود بعد تعديلهم، ثم تزكيتهم بشهادة المزكين؛ فإنه لا يتصور فيه النقض أصلاً وهو قضاء كامل موثق وثيق لا يحتمل البطلان والانتقاض. والنوع الثاني: المراد من النوع الثاني: القياس المبني على الوصف المتحد في الجنس بين الأصل والفرع، وكلا النوعين قد ثبتت علتيهما في الأصل بالنص الشافي: ص ۲۷۳] بمنزلة شهادة المستور: لأنه لم تظهر عدالته وفسقه كما لم يظهر كون الوصف علة بدليل من نص أو إجماع الممانعة : هي أساس المناظرة وأصلها؛ لأن المناظرة وضعت على مثال الخصومات في الدعاوي الواقعة في حقوق العباد، فالمعلل يدعي لزوم الحكم الذي رام قصد
Chapter on the questions directed at analogy. The questions directed at analogy are eight: 1. Opposition (al-mumāna'a), 2. Affirming the necessary consequence of the cause (al-qawl bi-mūjib al-'illa), 3. Inversion (al-qalb), 4. Reverse (al-'aks), 5. Invalidity of the relatum (fasād al-wad') = There exists a suitable descriptor in the original case other than the descriptor you gave as cause. An example of this is as al-Shafi'i said: Zakat is obligatory on the minor's property by analogy to the adult, and the commonality is fulfilling the need of the poor. The questioner can invalidate it by an appropriate difference, saying: The obligation of zakat in the consensus case is to purify sins and transgressions, and this meaning is absent in the branch case, so it is not obligatory – as in al-Fusūl and al-Ma'dan. 'The action was': i.e., according to what we mentioned of the three types. The difference between them: the descriptor known by the Book and Sunnah is like the tazkiya of the upright witness from the verificator, because the text's indication that it is a cause is like the tazkiya of the witness from the verificator. The descriptor known by consensus is like the witness whose integrity appeared before tazkiya, because consensus does not indicate explicitly or implicitly that this descriptor is a cause. The first type: it is the cause textually indicated by the Quran and hadith, like a judgment by the testimony of witnesses after their ta'dīl, then their tazkiya by the testimony of verificators; for it cannot be invalidated at all, and it is a complete, well-documented judgment that cannot be invalidated or overturned. The second type: the intended meaning of the second type is analogy built on a descriptor that is common in genus between original and branch, and both types have their causes established in the original by a definitive text? [p. 273] Like the testimony of the mastur (unknown character), because his integrity and immorality are not apparent, just as it is not apparent that the descriptor is a cause by evidence from text or consensus. Al-Mumāna'a: it is the foundation of debate and its origin, because debate was set up on the model of litigation in claims regarding the rights of people, so the mu'allil (proponent) claims the necessity of the ruling he intends.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a listing of the eight standard objections (as'ila) that can be raised against a qiyas argument in Hanafi usul. The eight are: (1) al-mumāna'a (denial or opposing the premise), (2) al-qawl bi-mūjib al-'illa (accepting the cause but denying its application), (3) al-qalb (inversion – applying the same reasoning to the opponent's case), (4) al-'aks (reverse – showing the opposite conclusion), and (5) fasād al-wad' (invalidity of the relatum – i.e., the original case or cause is flawed). An example is given of al-Shafi'i: he argued zakat on minor's property by analogy to an adult, with the commonality being fulfilling the poor's need. The questioner can rebut by an appropriate difference (farq munasib): the obligation in the adult case is to purify sins, which is absent in minors. The passage then distinguishes three types of descriptors based on how they are known: by text (Quran/hadith) – like a witness after tazkiya, cannot be overturned; by consensus – like a witness whose integrity appears but not yet verified; and by mere suitability – like a witness of unknown character (mastur). It explains that the eight objections are fundamental to debate, likening debate to litigation in civil disputes.

Key Terms
الممانعة (al-mumāna'a): The first objection: denying the opponent's claim (e.g., denying that the original case has a particular ruling or that the descriptor is found in the branch)
القول بموجب العلة (al-qawl bi-mūjib al-'illa): The second objection: accepting the existence of the cause but denying that it produces the ruling in the branch (e.g., admitting the illa but claiming another impediment)
القلب (al-qalb): Inversion: taking the same cause and applying it to the opponent's own case to produce a contradictory ruling
العكس (al-'aks): Reverse: showing that the opposite ruling is reached by applying the same method (e.g., if cause gives wujub, then its absence gives 'adam al-wujub)
فساد الوضع (fasād al-wad'): Invalidity of the relatum: pointing out that the chosen descriptor is not the true cause because a more suitable one exists in the original
شهادة المستور (shahādat al-mastūr): Testimony of an unknown witness: analogous to a descriptor known only by suitability, not via text or consensus

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first of the eight objections (as'ila) against qiyas?
A) Qalb
B) Qawl bi-mūjib al-'illa
C) Mumāna'a
D) 'Aks

Key Takeaway
The eight objections to qiyas, exemplified by the Hanafi critique of al-Shafi'i's zakat analogy, provide a systematic method to test analogical arguments, with the strength of the cause (text, consensus, or suitability) determining vulnerability to objection.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

The Eight Objections to Qiyas: A Structured Overview
Lists eight objections to qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and sequence the eight standard objections (as'ilah) to qiyas in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Eight objections challenge a qiyas; the first is prevention (mumana'ah).

The Hanafi usul tradition provides a structured procedure for debating qiyas. This lesson introduces the eight questions (as'ilah) that an opponent may raise, starting with prevention and moving through inversion, counter-argument, and others. Memorizing this sequence is key to understanding Hanafi legal argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list
Read the passage and find the eight objections enumerated. They are: al-mumana'ah, al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah, al-qalb, al-'aks, fasad al-wad', al-farq, al-naqd, and al-mu'aradah.
The Arabic terms are technical; note their order.
2
Memorize the sequence
The order is not random; it reflects the preferred progression in a debate. Mumana'ah is always first.
In al-Fusul it is stated that the questioner should not skip to other objections unless necessary.
3
Distinguish the objections
Briefly understand the meaning of each: prevention, requirement of the cause, inversion, opposite, invalidity of position, distinction, refutation, counter-argument.
You will study mumana'ah in depth in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأسئلة المتوجهة على القياس
الأَسْوَلَةُ المتوجهة على القياس ثمانية:
١ - الممانعةُ ٢- والقول بموجب العلة ٣- والقلبُ ٤ - والعكس ٥- وفساد الوَضع ٦ - والفرق ٧ - والنقض -۸- والمعارضة.
أمَّا الممانعة فنوعان: أحدهما: منع الوصف والثاني منع الحكم، مثاله: في قولهم: صَدَقَةُ الفطر وجبَتْ بالفطر فلا تسقط بموته ليلة الفطر.
قلنا: لا نسلم وجوبها بالفطر بل عندنا تحب برأس يمونه ويلي عليه.
وكذلك إذا قيل: قدرُ الزكاة واجب في الذمة فلا يسقط بهلاك النصاب كالدين. قلنا: لا نسلم أن قدر الزكاة واجبٌ في الذمة بل أداؤه واجب، ولئن قال : الواجب أداؤه، فلا يسقط بالهلاك كالدين بعد المطالبة.
أي قدر الزكاة
قلنا: لا نسلّمُ أن الأداء واجب في صورة الدين بل حَرم المنع حتى يخرج عن العُهدة بالتخلية، وهذا من قبيل منع الحكم.
= إثباته على السائل، والسائل يدعي عليه، فكان سبيله الإنكار كما أن سبيل المدعى عليه في الحقوق الدفع نفسه والإنكار ، فلا ينبغي له أن يتجاوز إلى غير الممانعة إلا عند الضرورة وهي أنه إذا ثبت ما ادعاه المجيب مؤثراً في الحكم يتجاوز السائل عنها إلى القول بموجب العلة إن أمكنه ذلك بأن كان الوصف من جنس الحكم
عن
وإلا يشتغل بالقلب، ثم بالمعارضة، فإذا زال الكلام إلى المعارضة سهل الأمر على المجيب كذا في "الفصول".
منع الوصف بأن يقول: لا نسلم أن الوصف الذي جعله المعلل علة موجود في المتنازع فيه، والمنع إما مع السند أو بدونه، والسند ما يكون المنع مبنياً عليه.
يمونه ويلي عليه: أي يقوم المكلف بكفايته ويتحمل مؤنته، وقوله: يلي عليه أي: ذلك المكلف على ذلك الرأس؛ لقوله عليه: "أدوا عمن "تمونون" أي تحملوا هذه المؤنة عمن وجب عليكم مؤنته، فعلم به أن الرأس سبب، وسيأتي تحقيقه في باب الأسباب من هذا الكتاب.
وكذلك أي مثل الممانعة في الوصف في المسألة السابقة الممانعة فيما إذا قال: قدر الزكاة وهو خمسة دراهم واجب في الذمة لا تعلق لها بالعين كذا في "المعدن".
فلا يسقط بهلاك إلخ: جعل الشافعي به وجوب مقدار الزكاة علة للحكم وهو بقاء الواجب بعد هلاك المال، وإنما تمنع هذه العلة فنقول: لا نسلم أن قدر الزكاة واجب في الذمة بل أداؤه واجب في الذمة كذا في "فصول" الحواشي.
وهذا أي قولنا: لا نسلم أن وجوب الأداء ثابت في صورة الدين من قبيل منع إلخ لأن وجوب الأداء وجوازه من قبيل الأحكام.
Section on the questions directed against qiyas
Questions directed against qiyas are eight:
1. Prevention (al-mumana'ah) 2. Saying according to the requirement of the cause 3. Inversion 4. Opposite 5. Invalidity of position 6. Distinction 7. Refutation 8. Counter-argument.
As for prevention, it is of two types: one: prevention of the description, and the second: prevention of the ruling. Example: in their statement: Sadaqat al-fitr is obligatory due to breaking the fast, so it does not fall away by his death on the night of fitr.
We said: We do not accept its obligation due to breaking the fast; rather, with us, it is obligatory for a head he supports and is responsible for.
Similarly, if it is said: The amount of zakat is obligatory in the dhimmah, so it does not fall away by destruction of the nisab, like a debt. We said: We do not accept that the amount of zakat is obligatory in the dhimmah; rather, its fulfillment is obligatory, and even if he says: what is obligatory is its fulfillment, then it does not fall away by destruction, like a debt after demand.
Meaning the amount of zakat.
We said: We do not accept that fulfillment is obligatory in the case of debt; rather, it is forbidden to prevent [payment] until he is discharged from liability by [the creditor's] release, and this falls under prevention of the ruling.
= Its establishment upon the questioner, and the questioner claims against him, so his way is denial, just as the way of the defendant in rights is the same denial and avoidance. So he should not go beyond prevention except out of necessity, namely, when what the respondent has claimed as effective in the ruling is established, the questioner goes beyond it to saying according to the requirement of the cause if possible, namely if the description is of the type of the ruling.
Otherwise, he engages in inversion, then counter-argument. When the discussion moves to counter-argument, the matter becomes easy for the respondent. So in 'al-Fusul'.
Prevention of the description: he says: We do not accept that the description made by the arguer as a cause exists in the contested case, and the prevention is either with evidence or without, and the evidence is what the prevention is based on.
'Yamunuhu wa yali 'alayhi': i.e., the responsible person provides for his maintenance and bears his expenses, and his saying 'yali 'alayhi' means: that responsible person is responsible for that head; because of his statement, peace be upon him: 'Pay sadaqah for those you maintain,' i.e., bear this expense for those whose maintenance is obligatory upon you. Thus it is known that the head is a cause, and its verification will come in the chapter of causes from this book.
Similarly, like prevention of the description in the previous question, prevention is when he says: The amount of zakat, which is five dirhams, is obligatory in the dhimmah, with no connection to the property. So in 'al-Ma'dan'.
'So it does not fall away by destruction, etc.': al-Shafi'i made the obligation of the amount of zakat a cause for the ruling, which is the survival of the obligation after destruction of the property, and it is this cause that we prevent by saying: We do not accept that the amount of zakat is obligatory in the dhimmah; rather, its fulfillment is obligatory in the dhimmah. So in 'Fusul al-Hawashi'.
And this, i.e., our statement: We do not accept that the obligation of fulfillment is established in the case of debt, falls under prevention, etc., because the obligation of fulfillment and its permissibility are among the categories of rulings.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage addresses the objections (as'ilah) that can be raised against a qiyas. The author enumerates eight standard objections used in debate. The first, mumana'ah (prevention), is further divided into two types: prevention of the description (man' al-wasf) and prevention of the ruling (man' al-hukm). The text provides two Hanafi-Shafi'i disputes as examples. In the first example, regarding sadaqat al-fitr, the Hanafi position is that the obligation is based on supporting the person (the head), not merely on breaking the fast. Therefore, the condition of the cause is denied. In the second example, regarding zakat, the Hanafis distinguish between the obligation of the amount in the dhimmah (which would survive destruction) and the obligation of payment (which may be affected by destruction). The text also explains the procedure for objections in a debate: the questioner should start with mumana'ah, then move to other objections only if necessary. The commentary cites 'al-Fusul' and 'al-Ma'dan' as references. A hadith from the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) is quoted: 'Pay sadaqah for those you maintain' to support the Hanafi position on fitrana.

Key Terms
ممانعة (mumana'ah): The first objection to qiyas, which prevents the acceptance of the description (wasf) or the ruling (hukm).
القلب (al-qalb): Inversion objection, where the opponent reverses the claimed cause to argue the opposite ruling.
المعارضة (al-mu'aradah): Counter-argument objection, where the opponent presents an alternative qiyas that conflicts with the original.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many objections (as'ilah) are typically directed against a qiyas according to this passage?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul tradition specifies eight objections to qiyas, beginning with mumana'ah (prevention).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

8 min
Mumana'ah (Prevention): First Objection and Its Two Types
Elaborates the first objection (mumana'ah) with two types and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of mumana'ah (man' al-wasf and man' al-hukm) and apply them to Hanafi-Shafi'i disputes on sadaqat al-fitr and zakat.

Core Idea
Mumana'ah has two forms: preventing the cause (wasf) or preventing the ruling (hukm).

The first objection, mumana'ah, prevents the opponent's qiyas by denying either the claimed cause (description) or the ruling itself. This lesson unpacks both types using two real Hanafi examples: the obligation of sadaqat al-fitr and the obligation of zakat. We also see the debate procedure: when to stay with mumana'ah and when to move to other objections.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types
Mumana'ah is of two types: man' al-wasf (prevention of the description/cause) and man' al-hukm (prevention of the ruling). The first denies that the claimed cause exists in the contested case; the second denies that the ruling applies despite the cause.
In the first example, the Hanafis prevent the description (cause = breaking the fast); in the second, they prevent the ruling (obligation of fulfillment).
2
Analyze the sadaqat al-fitr example
The Shafi'i argues that sadaqat al-fitr is obligatory due to breaking the fast (futur), so if the person dies on the night of fitr, the obligation drops. The Hanafi prevents the description: the cause is not breaking the fast but the head (ra's) one maintains, as per the hadith.
This is man' al-wasf because the Hanafis deny the cause.
3
Analyze the zakat example
The Shafi'i argues that the amount of zakat is obligatory in the dhimmah, so it does not fall away by destruction of the nisab, like a debt. The Hanafi prevents the ruling: what is obligatory is the fulfillment (ada'), not the amount itself, and fulfillment is not like a debt after demand.
This is man' al-hukm because the Hanafis deny that the ruling (obligation of the amount in dhimmah) is established.
4
Understand the debate procedure
The passage describes that the questioner should start with mumana'ah and only move to other objections (qawl bi-mujib al-illah, qalb, etc.) if necessary, when the respondent's claim is established.
This shows the structured nature of Hanafi usul debate.
5
Identify the dalil for the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position on fitrana is supported by the hadith: 'ادوا عمن تمونون' (Pay sadaqah for those you maintain). This indicates the cause is maintenance, not fasting.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but sufficient for deriving the cause.

منع الوصف (man' al-wasf): Prevention of the description: denying that the claimed cause exists in the contested case.
منع الحكم (man' al-hukm): Prevention of the ruling: denying that the ruling claimed by the qiyas is established.

Definition and Types of Man' (Objection) in Qiyas
Defining the two types of objections in qiyas with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between objection to the description (man‘ al-waṣf) and objection to the ruling (man‘ al-ḥukm) in qiyas, and apply each type to a given dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Objections in qiyas target either the description (cause) or the ruling itself—two distinct modes of defense.

In Hanafi jadal (dialectics), when a qiyas argument is presented, the questioner may object in two structured ways. This passage from the commentary on Uṣūl al-Shāshī defines those two types—objection to the description (man‘ al-waṣf) and objection to the ruling (man‘ al-ḥukm)—and illustrates them with real examples from fiqh disputes, such as the obligation of ṣadaqat al-fiṭr and the nature of zakah duty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's target
Determine whether the questioner denies the description (cause) or the ruling itself in a given qiyas exchange.
The description is the alleged ‘illa; the ruling is the legal effect.
2
Distinguish the two types
Using the examples from the text, classify each objection as man‘ al-waṣf or man‘ al-ḥukm.
In the sadaqat al-fitr example, the Hanafi denies that fasting is the cause → man‘ al-waṣf. In the zakah example, the denial that performance is obligatory in debt is about the ruling → man‘ al-ḥukm.
3
Trace the dialectical sequence
Note the progression: initial objection, then if necessary, moving to qalb (inversion) or mu‘āraḍah (counter-argument). The objection may be with or without a sanad (support).
The commentator explains that the questioner should not go beyond objection unless the respondent's claim is established.
4
Apply to a fiqh dispute
Given a new scenario (e.g., Hanafi vs Shafi'i on whether wudu' is nullified by touching a woman), determine which type of objection would be appropriate and why.
Consider whether the dispute is about the effective cause (touch) or about the ruling (nullification).

Source Text

البحث الرابع في القياس

أمَّا الممانعة فنوعان: أحدهما: منع الوصف والثاني منع الحكم، مثاله: في قولهم: صَدَقَةُ الفطر وجبَتْ بالفطر فلا تسقط بموته ليلة الفطر.
قلنا: لا نسلم وجوبها بالفطر بل عندنا تحب برأس يمونه ويلي عليه.
وكذلك إذا قيل: قدرُ الزكاة واجب في الذمة فلا يسقط بهلاك النصاب كالدين. قلنا: لا نسلم أن قدر الزكاة واجبٌ في الذمة بل أداؤه واجب، ولئن قال : الواجب أداؤه، فلا يسقط بالهلاك كالدين بعد المطالبة.

Chapter on the Objections Directed to Qiyas. The Fourth Research on Qiyas: 6 – al-Farq, 7 – al-Naqd, 8 – al-Mu‘āraḍah. As for al-Mumāna‘ah (objection), it is of two types: one is objection to the description (man‘ al-waṣf) and the second is objection to the ruling (man‘ al-ḥukm). Its example: in their statement: 'The charity of fast-breaking becomes obligatory by the fast, so it is not nullified by his death on the night of Fitr.' We say: We do not accept that it is obligatory by the fast; rather, according to us, it is loved/incumbent upon a head [i.e., a person] whom he supports and whose expenses he bears. Similarly, if it is said: 'The amount of zakah is a debt upon the liability, so it is not nullified by the destruction of the nisab, like a debt.' We say: We do not accept that the amount of zakah is a debt upon the liability; rather, its performance is obligatory. And if he says: 'The obligatory is performance, so it is not nullified by destruction, like a debt after demand.' (i.e., the amount of zakah). We say: We do not accept that performance is obligatory in the case of debt; rather, prohibition of withholding [exists] until one is released from responsibility by [the act of] assigning [the property]. And this falls under the category of objection to the ruling. = Proof is upon the questioner, and the questioner claims against him, so his way is denial just as the way of the defendant in rights is the same defense and denial. So it is not appropriate for him to go beyond objection except out of necessity, which is that if what the respondent claims is established as effective in the ruling, then the questioner moves beyond it to saying that the ruling follows the cause, if he can do so, such that the description is of the same type as the ruling. Otherwise, he engages in inversion (qalb), then in counter-argument (mu‘āraḍah). When the discussion reaches counter-argument, the matter becomes easier for the respondent. Thus in 'al-Fuṣūl'. Objection to the description (man‘ al-waṣf) is that he says: We do not accept that the description which the exponent of the cause (mu‘allil) made a cause is present in the contested case. And the objection is either with a support (sanad) or without, and the support is that on which the objection is based. 'Yamūnahu wa yalī ‘alayhi': i.e., the competent person undertakes his maintenance and bears his expenses. His saying 'yalī ‘alayhi' means: that competent person [is] upon that head; due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Pay charity on behalf of those whom you support.' i.e., bear this maintenance on behalf of those for whom you must provide maintenance. So it is known by this that the head is a cause, and its verification will come in the chapter on causes of this book. 'Wa kadhālika': i.e., similar to the objection to the description in the previous issue is the objection when it is said: 'The amount of zakah, which is five dirhams, is a debt upon the liability, having no connection to the property.' Thus in 'al-Ma‘din'. 'So it is not nullified by destruction etc.': Al-Shāfi‘ī made the obligation of the amount of zakah a cause for the ruling, which is the persistence of the obligatory duty after the destruction of the property. And this cause is objected to, so we say: We do not accept that the amount of zakah is a debt upon the liability; rather, its performance is obligatory upon the liability. Thus in 'Fuṣūl al-Ḥawāshī'. 'Wa hādhā': i.e., our statement: We do not accept that the obligation of performance is affirmed in the case of debt falls under the category of objection to the ruling etc., because the obligation and permissibility of performance fall under the category of rulings.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on the matn defines the two types of objection (man‘) in qiyas: objection to the description (man‘ al-waṣf) and objection to the ruling (man‘ al-ḥukm). The first example involves the charity of fast-breaking (ṣadaqat al-fiṭr): the opponent claims it becomes obligatory by fasting and thus should not be nullified by death on the night of Fitr. The Hanafi response denies that it becomes obligatory by fasting; rather, it is a duty upon the head of the household based on maintenance. The second example concerns zakah: the opponent claims the amount of zakah is a debt upon the liability, so destruction of the nisab does not nullify it. The Hanafi response distinguishes: the performance (adā’) of zakah is obligatory, not the amount itself as a debt. Further, even if performance is obligatory, in the case of debt after demand, it is not nullified by destruction, but this is a different ruling. The commentator explains the procedure for objections: the questioner's initial recourse is denial, and he should not go beyond objection unless necessary; then he may counter by affirming the cause, then engage in qalb (inversion) or mu‘āraḍah (counter-argument). The text also clarifies linguistic points from hadith regarding maintenance.

Key Terms
منع الوصف (man‘ al-waṣf): objection to the description: denying that the description alleged as cause exists in the contested case
منع الحكم (man‘ al-ḥukm): objection to the ruling: denying the ruling in the original case or its transfer to the branch
الممانعة (al-mumāna‘ah): the act of objecting in a qiyas argument; of two types

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi response to the claim about sadaqat al-fitr, what is the basis for the obligation?
A) Fasting
B) The head of household's maintenance
C) The amount of zakat
D) The death of the person

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi jadal, the questioner must first target the cause (man‘ al-waṣf) or the ruling (man‘ al-ḥukm); only then, if necessary, may he proceed to qalb or mu‘āraḍah.

Analogy on Tathlith in Wiping the Head: Hanafi Response
Dispute on applying tathlith sunna to wiping via qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of man' al-hukm to critique a qiyas argument and distinguish between elongation (itaala) and repetition in acts of worship.

Core Idea
The sunnah in pillars is elongation, not repetition; repetition is only a substitute when elongation is impossible.

In this passage from Usul al-Shashi, a dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is on whether wiping the head three times is sunnah is resolved through careful analysis of the nature of completion in acts of worship. The Shafi'i argues by qiyas from washing the limbs, but the Hanafi denies the validity of the analogy by showing that the assumed effective cause is not present in the base case. This lesson unpacks the dialectical reasoning and the Hanafi principle that the goal is elongation, not mere repetition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogical argument
Recognize how the Shafi'i constructs a qiyas: washing is a pillar and tathlith is sunnah; wiping is also a pillar, so tathlith should be sunnah there. The inferred cause ('illa) is 'pillarhood' or 'being a rukn'.
The Shafi'i assumes that the sunnah status transfers via the shared attribute of pillarhood.
2
Locate the Hanafi response: man' al-hukm
The Hanafi denies (man') that tathlith is sunnah in the base case (washing). They argue that the sunnah is actually elongation of the act, not repetition per se.
Man' al-hukm is a standard method to break the analogy by attacking the ruling in the asl.
3
Understand the distinction: elongation vs. repetition
In all pillars, completion (takmil) is achieved by elongating the act (e.g., longer standing, bowing). Repetition is only a means in washing because washing covers the entire limb, making elongation impossible without multiple passes. In wiping, elongation can be achieved by a single thorough wipe (istiya'ab).
The underlying principle is that the sunnah is qualitative (elongation), not quantitative (repetition).
4
See how wiping allows elongation without repetition
Since wiping can cover the entire head in one motion, the goal of elongation is attained by doing it thoroughly once. There is no need for three separate wipes.
Thus the Hanafi hold that the sunnah for wiping is one complete wipe (istiya'ab).
5
Evaluate the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis wipe once thoroughly; Shafi'is wipe three times. This difference stems from contrasting views on the nature of sunnah completion.
Understanding the rationale helps avoid blind imitation and appreciate the reasoning behind each madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك إذا قال: المسحُ ركن في باب الوضوء فليسَن تثليثه كالغَسْل. مسح الرأس المسح قلنا: لا نُسَلّم أن التثليث مسنون في الغسل، بل إطالة الفعل في محل الفرض زيادةً على المفروض، كإطالة القيام والقراءة في القيام والركوع والسجود إنما يكون تكميلها بإطالتها لا بتكرارها، وكذا القراءة إلا أنا لم نجد محل الإطالة في الغسل؛ لأن المفروض لما استغرق محله كانت الإطالة تكميلاً في غير محل الفرض فصرنا ضرورة إلى And likewise, if one says: Wiping the head is a pillar in the chapter of ablution, so its trine repetition is sunnah like washing. . Wiping the head. The wiping. We said: We do not concede that trine repetition is sunnah in washing, rather it is elongation of the act in the place of the obligatory beyond what is obligatory, like elongation of standing and recitation in the chapter of prayer, except that elongation in the chapter of washing is not conceivable except by repetition because the act covers the entire place, and by the same token we say in the chapter of wiping that elongation is sunnah by way of thorough coverage. . The wiping etc.: So the proponent from the Shafi'is for example reasoned the ruling of sunnah status of trine repetition in the washed limbs by the pillarhood, that trine repetition in the obligatory was only due to the fact that washing is obligatory and a pillar of ablution, and the obligatory is completed by sunan and completion is only by repetition and its perfection by trine; then when this cause, i.e. obligatory-ness, is found in wiping, its completion by trine is also sunnah. This is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i... that it is sunnah with different waters, stated in his books and affirmed by a group of the majority of his companions, but al-Rafi'i reported that its being once is a view of our companions and it is the madhhab of most scholars and jurists, and al-Tirmidhi also reported it from al-Shafi'i, and his madhhab on trine repetition was reported by Ibn al-Mundhir from Anas, Sa'id ibn Jubayr, and 'Ata', and it is a narration from Ahmad and Dawud. The Hanafi madhhab is that the sunnah is the single thorough wiping; Ibn al-Mundhir said, and among those who held it are Ibn 'Umar, Talhah ibn Musarrif, al-Hakam, Hammad, al-Nakha'i, Mujahid, Salim ibn 'Abdillah, al-Hasan al-Basri, Malik, Ahmad, al-Thawri, and others, and Ibn al-Mundhir chose it. Ibn 'Adi said: All the narrators said that wiping the head is once and it is the most correct. So in al-Husul. We said 'We do not concede' etc. So we prevented the ruling, i.e., the sunnah of trine in the analogue (washing of the three limbs), and its explanation: that repetition is not an intended sunnah in the original; because the attribute of pillarhood has no effect on repetition, rather its effect is on the sunnah of completion; because the sunan and obligations were only legislated as completions for the obligations, and because completion is the principle in all other pillars, and completion is only by elongating the obligatory in its place as much as possible. Do you not see that standing, bowing, and prostration are completed by elongating them, not by repeating them, and similarly recitation, except that we did not find a place for elongation in washing; because when the obligatory occupies its entire place, elongation would be completion outside the place of the obligatory, so we were necessarily driven to repetition as a substitute for the original, and acting by the original is possible in wiping the head, so we said: by elongation through thorough coverage. Understand. So in al-Fusul. 'Beyond the obligatory': i.e., in its place; and that is because repetition is not an intended sunnah in the original, i.e., in washing; because the attribute of pillarhood has no effect on repetition, rather its effect is on the sunnah of completion; because the sunan and obligations were only legislated as completions for the obligations; because completion is the principle in all other pillars, and completion is only by elongating the obligatory in its place beyond the obligatory amount. So in al-Ma'din. 'Except that elongation' etc. is an answer to what is said: If elongation is sunnah in washing without repetition, then why was elongation not performed in it? So he answered that elongation in the chapter of washing is not conceivable except by repetition, because the act covers the entire place. So in al-Ma'din. It is not conceivable except by repetition because the act covers the place, and by the same token we say in the chapter of wiping that elongation is sunnah by way of thorough coverage.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (ikhtilaf) between the Hanafis and Shafi'is on whether tathlith (three times) is sunnah for wiping the head (mash) by qiyas on the washed limbs. The Shafi'i argues: since washing the arms, face, feet is a pillar (rukn) and tathlith is sunnah there, and wiping the head is also a pillar, the same ruling should apply. The Hanafi response uses the method of man' al-hukm (denying the ruling in the base case). They argue that in washing, the sunnah is not repetition per se but elongation (itala) of the act. Since washing covers the entire limb, elongation can only be achieved by repetition. In wiping the head, elongation is possible by a single thorough wipe (istiya'b), so repetition is not required. The Hanafi position is that the sunnah for wiping is one complete wipe covering the entire head. The commentary expands on this by citing various authorities and clarifying the reasoning: pillarhood entails completion (takmil), and completion is normally by elongation, not repetition. The necessity of repetition in washing is due to the impossibility of elongation otherwise. The opponent's objection that elongation should be applied to wiping is answered by stating that wiping does allow elongation through thorough covering.

Key Terms
التثليث (al-tathlith): Performing an act three times, specifically in the context of washing or wiping in wudu
الإطالة (al-itaala): Elongating an obligatory act beyond its minimum, as a means of completion (takmil)
الاستيعاب (al-istiyaab): Covering the entire part to be wiped, i.e., the whole head, without gaps

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Wiping three times
B) Wiping once thoroughly
C) Wiping with wet hands
D) Wiping from front to back

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms that the sunnah in worship pillars is elongation, not repetition; repetition is only a substitute when elongation is impossible.

The Condition of Possession in Food Sales: A Usul Dispute on Qiyas
Usul dispute on qabd requirement in food sales via qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse the Hanafi objection to qiyas that requires qabd in food sales by identifying the man' al-hukm argument and explaining the distinction between specification (ta'yin) and possession (qabd).

Core Idea
The Hanafi school denies qabd as a condition in food sales; the real condition is specification, which is achieved without possession.

This passage from Usul al-Shashi presents a usul dispute where an opponent analogises food sales to currency exchange, arguing that mutual possession (taqabud) is a condition. The Hanafi response refutes the analogy by contesting the identification of the condition. Instead, they argue that the condition is specification (ta'yin) to prevent the prohibited sale of credit for credit (bay' al-nasi'ah). Food, unlike currency, is specified by contract alone, so possession is unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Qiyas
Recognise that the opponent draws an analogy between food sales and currency exchange, claiming that both are ribawi and therefore require qabd. What is the apparent commonality (jami')?
The jami' is that both are ribawi goods (māl yajrī fīhi al-ribā).
2
Locate the Hanafi Response: Man' al-Hukm
The Hanafi response begins with 'la nusallim' (we do not concede). This is a rejection of the opponent's claim that qabd is a condition in currency. What do they assert instead?
They assert that the condition is specification (ta'yin), not possession.
3
Understand the Distinction between Food and Currency
Why is specification achieved without possession in food but not in currency? The text explains that currencies like dirhams and dinars are considered as debts in the liability, so they are not specified until possession. Food items, however, are specified by the contract itself.
This difference is crucial to the Hanafi argument.
4
Analyse the Role of Hadith
The passage references the hadith 'hand to hand' (yadan bi-yadin) and 'like for like' from Muslim. How does the Hanafi interpretation differ from the opponent's?
The Hanafi school interprets 'hand to hand' as requiring specification to prevent riba, not necessarily physical qabd.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
What is the practical consequence of this dispute? In a food-for-food sale, the Hanafi school rules that the contract is valid without immediate possession, while the Shafi'i school requires qabd.
This difference affects the validity of spot and deferred food exchanges.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يقال: التقابض في بيع الطعام بالطعام شرطٌ كالنقود، قلنا: لا نسلم أن التقابض شرطٌ في باب النقود، بل الشرط تعيينها؛ كيلا يكونَ بيعَ النَّسِيئَةِ بالنَّسِيئَة، غيرَ أن النقود لا تتعيَّن إلا بالقبض عندنا. بيع الطعام إلخ أي من جنس الحبوب كالحنطة والشعير والظاهر من هذا تماثلهما في الجنس كبيع الحنطة بالحنطة والملح بالملح، ويشترط فيه التسوية بحديث الربا مثلاً بمثل أخرجه مسلم، وعلى هذا الظاهر يشترط التقابض أيضاً بحديث الربا، وفيه: يداً بيد"، وقوله: شرط أي واجب ضروري، وإلا فلا تعليل لإثبات الشرطية، وإنما هو للحكم على ما تقرر. كالنقود: أي بيع النقود وهي الأثمان حيث شرط تقابض البدلين في عقد الصرف، والجامع: أن كلاً منهما مال يجري فيه الربا. بيعَ النَّسيئَةِ إلخ وهو حرام لنهيه عليها عن بيع الكالي بالكالي أي النسيئة بالنسيئة، ويوئيده أحاديث آخر أيضاً كحديث يداً بيد، وقال أحمد محله قد أجمع الناس على عدم جواز بيع الدين بالدين. غير أن النقود إلخ: جواب ما يقال لما كان التعيين في النقود شرطاً دون القبض، فينبغي أن يجوز بيع النقود بدون القبض، فأجاب بأن النقود لا تتعين وإن عنيت إلا بالقبض كذا في "معدن الأصول". إلا بالقبض: إذ الدراهم والدنانير لا يتعينان في العقود والفسوخ؛ لثبوتها في الذمة، ولهذا إذا ابتاع سلعة بدراهم معينة جاز أن يوجب مكانها أخرى، بخلاف الطعام فإنه يتعين بالتعيين من غير قبض، فلا يحتاج إلى التقابض كذا في "المعدن".
And likewise it is said: Mutual taking possession in the sale of food for food is a condition like in currencies. We said: We do not concede that mutual taking possession is a condition in the chapter of currencies, rather the condition is their specification; so that it should not be a sale of credit for credit; except that currencies are not specified except by possession according to us. Sale of food etc.: i.e., from the type of grains like wheat and barley, and the apparent is that they are similar in genus like sale of wheat for wheat and salt for salt, and equivalence is required in it by the hadith of riba 'like for like' as narrated by Muslim, and on this apparent meaning mutual taking possession is also required by the hadith of riba, in which it says 'hand to hand', and his statement 'condition' means obligatory necessary, otherwise there is no attribution of a ruling to establish conditionality, rather it is for the ruling as established. Like currencies: i.e., the sale of currencies which are prices where mutual taking possession of the two substitutes is required in a currency exchange contract, and the commonality is that each of them is property in which riba is applicable. Sale of credit etc. and it is prohibited due to the prohibition on selling credit for credit, i.e., postponement for postponement, and it is supported by other hadiths like 'hand to hand', and Ahmad said: its place is that the people have agreed on the impermissibility of selling debt for debt. Except that currencies etc.: an answer to what is said: Since specification is condition for currencies and not possession, it should be permissible to sell currencies without possession. So he answered that currencies are not specified, even if intended, except by possession. So in Ma'dan al-Usul. Except by possession: Since dirhams and dinars are not specified in contracts and dissolutions because they are established in the liability, and for this reason if one buys a commodity with specified dirhams, it is permissible to substitute others for them, unlike food which is specified by specification without possession, so it does not need mutual taking possession. So in al-Ma'din.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents a second example of an objection (su'al) directed against qiyas. The opponent argues that mutual taking possession (taqabud) in the sale of food for food is a condition by analogy with currency exchange (sarf). The Hanafi response again uses man' al-hukm: they deny that taqabud is a condition in currency; rather the condition is the specification (ta'yin) of the coins to avoid the prohibited sale of credit for credit. However, in practice, specification of coins is achieved only by physical possession (qabd) according to the Hanafi view. So the opponent's analogy misidentifies the cause. The commentary explains that food, being fungible, is specified by mere verbal specification without possession, so taqabud is not needed. The key distinction is that dirhams and dinars are considered as debts in the liability until possession, so they are not specified without possession, whereas food items are specified immediately upon contract. The Hanafi position is that in food sales, taqabud is not a condition; the condition is specification, which occurs without possession.

Key Terms
التقابض (al-taqabud): Mutual physical taking possession of the two exchanged goods in a sale contract
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specification of the subject matter in a contract, making it uniquely identified
بيع النسيئة (bay' al-nasi'ah): Sale with deferred payment, specifically used here for credit-for-credit sale

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, what is the real condition in currency exchange that also prevents riba?
A) qabd (possession)
B) ta'yin (specification)
C) equality of amounts
D) both A and B

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school contests qiyas by denying the premise (man' al-hukm), arguing that the condition in currency is specification, not possession, and that food is specified without possession.

Qawl bi-Mujab al-Illa: Admitting the Cause but Denying the Effect
Definition of a method of objection in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qawl bi-mujab al-illa and apply it to identify a valid objection to a qiyas argument.

Core Idea
You can accept the opponent's cause yet defeat their qiyas by denying the claimed effect.

In Hanafi usul, qiyas can be challenged in several ways. One subtle but powerful method is to concede that the attribute identified as the cause (illa) is correct, but then argue that the ruling the opponent derives from it does not actually follow. This is called qawl bi-mujab al-illa. Mastering this tool sharpens your ability to dissect legal analogies.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of a qiyas argument
A qiyas has four components: asl, far', illa, and hukm. The illa is the attribute that connects the original case to the new case. The objector can attack any component.
2
Distinguish qawl bi-mujab al-illa from other objections
Unlike man' al-illa (denying the cause altogether), qawl bi-mujab al-illa accepts the cause but contests the claimed effect (malul). The objector says: 'Even if your cause is valid, the ruling you claim does not follow from it.'
This is a more refined objection that forces the proponent to clarify the entailment.
3
Analyze the wudu example
The passage gives an example: The proponent argues that because the elbow is a boundary (hadd), it does not enter under the washing (i.e., it is excluded). The objector concedes that 'boundary' is a cause, but points out that the claimed effect (non-inclusion) is not necessarily entailed by boundary; in fact, boundaries can be included.
The Hanafi position is that the elbow must be washed, so the claimed effect is incorrect.
4
Identify the key terms
Learn the Arabic terms: al-qawl bi-mujab al-illa, al-illa, al-malul, al-mirfaq. Understanding these terms helps you follow usul texts.
5
Apply the concept in a new case
Given a qiyas argument, test whether a qawl bi-mujab al-illa objection is possible: identify the illa, admit it, then ask whether the claimed hukm necessarily follows.
If the hukm does not follow, you have successfully used this objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول بموجب العلة : فهو تسليم كون الوصف علة، وبيان أن معلولها غير ما ادعاه المعلل. ومثاله: المرفَقُ حدٌ في باب الوضوء، فلا يدخُلُ تحت الغسل؛ لأن الحد لا يدخل . القول بموجب العلة المرفق أي الغاية
As for qawl bi-mujab al-illa (admitting the effective cause), it is conceding that the attribute is a cause, and showing that its effect is different from what the proponent claimed. Its example: The elbow is a boundary in the chapter of ablution, so it does not enter under washing; because the boundary does not enter. . Qawl bi-mujab al-illa. The elbow, i.e., the end point.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of objection to qiyas: qawl bi-mujab al-illa. The objector admits that the attribute (wasf) identified by the proponent is indeed the effective cause (illa), but argues that the ruling (malul) the proponent claims does not actually follow from that cause. The example given is from wudu: the elbow (mirfaq) is a limit (hadd) or boundary of the washing area. The proponent might argue that because the elbow is a boundary, it is not included in the washing (i.e., washing the arm ends at the elbow, not including it). The objector concedes that boundary is a cause, but points out that the effect (not entering under washing) is different from what the proponent claimed? Actually the text is brief: 'the elbow is a boundary, so it does not enter under washing because the boundary does not enter.' This appears to be the proponent's argument. The objector says: we admit that boundary is a cause, but the effect you claim is that the elbow does not enter washing; but perhaps the effect is something else? The passage is truncated, but the definition is clear. The Hanafi usul uses this as a valid criticism of qiyas: even if you accept the illa, the ruling derived may be incorrect. In this example, arguably the elbow is included in washing despite being a boundary, so the claimed effect is wrong.

Key Terms
القول بموجب العلة (al-qawl bi-mujab al-illa): Conceding that the attribute is a valid cause (illa) but arguing that the effect (malul) claimed is incorrect
المعلول (al-malul): The legal ruling that is said to follow from the cause (illa)
المرفق (al-mirfaq): The elbow; in wudu, it is the limit of washing the arm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the wudu example from the lesson, what effect (malul) does the objector deny?
A) The elbow is a boundary
B) The elbow does not enter under washing
C) The cause (illa) is valid
D) Washing includes the elbow

Key Takeaway
Qawl bi-mujab al-illa is a powerful objection: you can accept the opponent's cause yet still refute their qiyas by showing the claimed ruling does not follow.

Al-Qawl bi-Mujib al-‘Illah: Objection and Refutation
Defining the objection with an elbow-example refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah, identify its components, and apply the Hanafi refutation to the elbow-in-wudu example.

Core Idea
Accepting the cause but disputing the effect – a precise refutation requires identifying the correct ma'lul.

In usul al-fiqh debates, not all objections deny the cause. A sophisticated opponent may concede that your description is indeed a valid cause but argue that its legal effect is different from what you claim. This is 'al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah'. The classic Hanafi example involves the elbow in wudu, where the opponent says it is a limit and therefore not washed. The Hanafi response shows the opponent misidentified which limited the elbow belongs to. Understanding this objection sharpens your ability to evaluate and construct analogies.

Guided Path
1
Define the objection
Read the definition: al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah is when the opponent admits that the description is a valid cause (illah) but claims that its effect (ma'lul) is different from what the proponent asserts. Note the key terms: 'taslim' (admission) and 'bayān' (demonstration).
2
Analyse the opponent's argument
The opponent gives an example: the elbow is a limit in wudu. Since a limit is not included in the limited, the elbow should not be washed. Identify the opponent's claimed illah (being a limit) and their claimed ma'lul (exclusion from washing).
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response (qulnā) reclassifies the limit: the elbow is the limit of the dropped part (saqit), not the washed part. Therefore, the ruling of the dropped part does not apply – the elbow is indeed washed. Understand why this shifts the ma'lul correctly.
4
Identify the successful refutation
The Hanafi argument does not deny the opponent's general principle (limit not entering the limited). Instead, it shows that the opponent applied the principle to the wrong object. This is a classic example of exposing a misapplied qiyas by clarifying the correct ma'lul.
5
Generalise the lesson
Whenever you encounter this objection in usul debates, ask: 'What does the opponent identify as the ma'lul, and is it truly the result of the cause they accept?' The Hanafi response teaches meticulous attention to the precise scope of the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول بموجب العلة : فهو تسليم كون الوصف علة، وبيان أن معلولها غير ما ادعاه المعلل. ومثاله: المرفَقُ حدٌ في باب الوضوء، فلا يدخُلُ تحت الغسل؛ لأن الحد لا يدخل في المحدود. قلنا: المرفق حدُّ الساقط، فلا يدخُلُ تحت حكم الساقط؛ لأن الحد لا يدخل في المحدود.
As for al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah: it is the admission that the description is a cause, and the demonstration that its effect is other than what the proponent of the cause claimed. Its example: the elbow is a limit in the chapter of wudu, so it does not enter under the washing because the limit does not enter into the limited. We said: the elbow is the limit of the dropped part, so it does not enter under the ruling of the dropped part because the limit does not enter into the limited.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of objection to qiyas known as 'al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah' (accepting the necessary consequence of the cause). The opponent concedes that the description (wasf) is indeed a valid cause (illah) but argues that its legal effect (ma'lul) is different from what the proponent of qiyas claims. The example given is the elbow (mirfaq) in wudu. The opponent says the elbow is a limit (hadd) in wudu, so it should not be washed (under the ruling of washing the arms) because the limit is not included in what is limited. The Hanafi response refutes this: the elbow is the limit of the part that is dropped (saqit), meaning the limit of the area that is not washed, not the limit of the washed area. Since the limit is not included in the limited, the elbow is not included in the ruling of that which is dropped—i.e., it is indeed washed. Thus, the opponent's claimed effect is incorrect; the elbow is not excluded from washing. This shows a precise refutation based on the correct identification of the ma'lul.

Key Terms
القول بموجب العلة (al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah): An objection where the opponent accepts the cause but claims the effect is different.
المعلول (al-ma'lul): The legal effect or ruling that supposedly results from the cause.
الساقط (as-saqit): The part of the arm that is dropped from washing in wudu (i.e., the upper arm).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the source text, what does the Hanafi response (qulnā) use to refute the opponent's objection?
A) Denying that the elbow is a limit.
B) Reclassifying the elbow as the limit of the dropped part.
C) Arguing that the principle 'al-hadd la yadkhulu fi al-mahdud' is false.
D) Claiming that the elbow is not a limit in wudu.

Key Takeaway
Al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah is a refutation that accepts the cause but corrects the claimed effect; the Hanafi elbow example teaches precise identification of the ma'lul.

When the Law Specifies, No Personal Intention Needed
Using the same objection to argue that Ramadan fasts do not need individual intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on ta'yin (specification) to distinguish between acts specified by the Law and those that require personal intention.

Core Idea
When the Law specifies the act, the servant need not specify it; when it does not, the servant must intend it.

In this passage, we encounter a classic usul objection—al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah. The opponent argues that because both Ramadan and make-up fasts are obligatory, they should both require personal intention. The Hanafi response reveals a critical distinction: the Law's own specification can substitute for the servant's intention. Understanding this distinction is key to correct istinbat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection (al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah)
The opponent claims that if fasting Ramadan requires intention, it must require personal specification (ta'yin) just like make-up fasts, because both are obligatory.
This is a common argument by analogy that the Hanafi respond to by pointing out a difference in the 'illa.
2
State the Hanafi position on Ramadan vs qada'
The Hanafi hold that Ramadan does not require personal ta'yin, but qada' does.
The distinction is based on whether the Law has provided specification.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i opposing dalil
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that every obligatory act requires the servant's intention to specify which act is being performed.
The Shafi'i view treats all obligatory acts alike.
4
Examine the Hanafi response: why specification by Law eliminates need for ta'yin
The Hanafi response: The need for personal ta'yin arises only when the act is not already specified by the Law. Ramadan is specified by its time, so no personal ta'yin is needed.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a person fasting on a day in Ramadan without specifically intending 'Ramadan' has a valid fast according to the Hanafi school, but not according to the Shafi'i school.
This shows how a difference in usul leads to different fiqh rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يقال: صوم رمضان صوم فرض فلا يجوز بدون التعيين كالقضاء. قلنا: صوم الفرض لا يجوز بدون التعيين إلا أنه وجد التعيين ههنا من جهة الشرع. ولئن قال: صوم رمضان لا يجوز بدون التعيين من العبد كالقضاء. الشافعي قلنا: لا يجوز القضاء بدون التعيين، إلا أن التعيين لم يثبت من جهة الشرع، فلذلك يشترط تعيين العبد. وهنا وجد التعيين من جهة الشرع فلا يشترط تعيين العبد.
And likewise it is said: The fast of Ramadan is an obligatory fast, so it is not valid without specification (ta'yin) like the make-up fast. We said: Obligatory fast is not valid without specification, but specification has occurred here from the side of the Law. And if he said: The fast of Ramadan is not valid without specification from the servant, like the make-up fast (according to al-Shafi'i). We said: The make-up fast is not valid without specification, but specification has not been established from the side of the Law, therefore specification by the servant is required. And here specification has been found from the side of the Law, so specification by the servant is not required.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents another instance of the objection 'al-qawl bi-mujib al-illah'. The opponent (identified as al-Shafi'i) argues that since both Ramadan fasts and make-up fasts (qada') are obligatory, they should both require the servant's intention (niyyah) to specify which fast is being observed. The Hanafi response distinguishes: The Law itself has specified Ramadan by designating its time, so no additional personal specification is needed to differentiate it from other fasts. In contrast, make-up fasts are not time-bound; the Law does not specify them, so the servant must intend which missed fast he is fulfilling. Thus, the claimed similarity between the two is invalid, and the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
التعيين (al-ta'yin): Specification by the servant of which specific act of worship he is performing
صوم القضاء (sawm al-qada'): A make-up fast for a missed obligatory fast

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why does fasting Ramadan not require the servant to specify it with intention?
A) Because it is a voluntary fast.
B) Because the Law itself has specified Ramadan.
C) Because the servant always intends Ramadan.
D) Because make-up fasts do not require specification either.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between acts specified by the Law (e.g., Ramadan) and those left to the servant (e.g., qada'); only the latter require personal intention (ta'yin).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
