The Objection of Qalb (Inversion) in Qiyas: Definition and Types
Defining qalb and its two types with examples from riba and the Haram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qalb, identify its two types, and apply its condition to distinguish valid from invalid inversions in qiyas arguments.

Core Idea
Qalb reverses cause and effect in a qiyas argument, but only if the cause is a ruling, not a pure description.

In this passage, al-Shashi explains the objection of qalb (inversion) in qiyas, a tool used to invalidate an opponent's causal reasoning. He distinguishes two linguistic senses and two technical senses, and emphasizes a critical condition: qalb only applies when the cause is a ruling (hukm), not a descriptive attribute (wasf). Understanding this condition is key to correctly applying the objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two linguistic meanings of qalb
The word qalb literally means turning something upside down or inside out, which parallels its technical use in qiyas.
2
Distinguish the two technical types of qalb in qiyas
Type 1: swapping cause and effect (making the cause an effect and vice versa). Type 2: changing the form of the evidence to a state contrary to its original.
Both revert to the same idea of altering the causal relationship.
3
Understand the condition for valid qalb
Qalb is valid only when the cause (al-mu'allil) is a ruling (hukm), not a pure description (wasf). If the cause is a pure description, it cannot become a ruling, and thus inversion is impossible.
This condition is emphasized in the passage and distinguishes valid from invalid objections.
4
Apply qalb to the riba and Haram examples
For riba: the opponent claims large quantity causes small quantity; qalb inverts to small causing large. For Haram: Shafi'i claims prohibition of killing causes prohibition of cutting limbs; Hanafi inverts the relationship.
In both, the cause is a ruling (hukm), so qalb is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القلب فنوعان: أحدهما: أن يجعل ما جعله المعلل علة للحكم معلولاً لذلك الحكم. ومثاله في الشرعيات: جَرَيانُ الربا في الكثير يوجب جريانه في القليل كالأثمان، الحفنة فيحرم بيع من الطعام بالحفنتين منه. قلنا: لا بل جريان الربا في القليل يوجب جريانه في الكثير كالأثمان. أي ليس الأمر كذلك وكذلك في مسألة الملتجىء بالحَرَم: حرمة إتلاف النفس يوجب حرمَةَ إتلاف الطرف وأما القلب: هو في اللغة يستعمل في معنيين: أحدهما: أن يجعل أسفل الشيء أعلاه وأعلاه أسفله كقلب القصعة والكوز. وثانيهما: أن يجعل باطن الشيء ظاهره وظاهره باطنه كقلب الجراب والثوب، وكلاهما يرجعان إلى شيء واحد وهو تغيير هيأة الشيء على خلاف الهيأة التي كان عليها، فكذا في القياس يستعمل القلب الصحيح بمعنيين، وكلاهما يرجعان إلى معنى واحد وهو تغيير الدليل إلى هيأة تخالف التي كان عليها كذا قيل. معلولاً لذلك إلخ: يعني ما جعله المعلل علة جعله السائل معلولاً أي حكماً، وما جعله المعلل جعله السائل علة، وفيه إبطال التعليل بإبطال علته بجعلها حكماً، وإنما يصح هذا النوع من القلب فيما إذا علل المستدل بالحكم بأن جعل ما كان حكماً في الأصل علة لحكم،آخر فأما لو علل بالوصف المحض لا يرد عليه هذا القلب؛ لأن الوصف لا يصير حكماً بوجه بوجه ولا يصير الحكم الثابت علة له؛ لأنه سابق على الحكم كذا في "المعدن". بالحفنتين منه إلا من جعل الربا في الكثير حراماً في بيع ما يدخل تحت الكيل يوجب جريان الربا في القليل كالأثمان، فإنه يجري فيه الربا في القليل والكثير والجامع أن كلا منهما يجري فيه الربا. بل جريان الربا إلخ أي فقد قلبنا تعليل الخصم وجعلنا جريانه في الكثير حكما وهو علة في قياس الخصم، وجعلنا جريانه في القليل علة وهو حكم في قياسه وفيه أن القلب إنما يكون في الأصل والمصنف له قلب في الفرع، ويجاب بأن القلب في الفرع يستلزم القلب في الأصل كذا في "المعدن". الملتجيء بالحَرَم: وهو من عليه القصاص في النفس، فإنه إذا التجى إلى الحرم لا يقتل فيه عندنا؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمنا الآية، إلا أنه لا يطعم ولا يسقى ولا يجالس ولا يباع حتى يضطر إلى الخروج، فيقتل خارج الحرم، وعند الشافعي له: يقتل في الحرم بالقياس على من عليه القصاص في الطرف؛ لأنه إذا التجى إلى الحرم يستوفي منه القصاص اتفاقاً، عليه القصاص في النفس والجامع أن كلاً منهما ،جان قال أصحاب الشافعي له حرمة إتلاف النفس يوجب حرمة إتلاف الطرف يعني أن حرمة إتلاف النفس علة لحرمة إتلاف الطرف كصيد الحرم؛ لأن = فكذا
As for inversion (qalb), it is of two types: One is to make what the causer made a cause for the ruling into an effect (i.e., ruling) for that same ruling. Its example in legal matters: The occurrence of riba in a large quantity necessitates its occurrence in a small quantity like precious metals, the handful, so the sale of food for two handfuls of it is prohibited. We said: No, rather the occurrence of riba in a small quantity necessitates its occurrence in a large quantity like precious metals. That is not the case. Similarly in the issue of the one seeking refuge in the Haram: The prohibition of taking life necessitates the prohibition of taking a limb. As for qalb: it is used linguistically in two senses: one is to make the bottom of a thing its top and its top its bottom, like turning over a bowl or a jug. The second is to make the inside of a thing its outside and its outside its inside, like turning a bag or a garment. Both revert to one thing: changing the state of a thing contrary to the state it was upon. So too in qiyas, sound qalb is used in two senses, both reverting to one meaning: changing the evidence to a state contrary to what it was upon, as it is said. 'Effect for that ruling etc.': meaning what the causer made a cause, the questioner makes an effect (i.e., ruling), and what the causer made a ruling, the questioner makes a cause. In it is the invalidation of the causal reasoning by invalidating its cause by making it a ruling. This type of qalb is only valid when the arguer reasons with a ruling by making what was a ruling in the original a cause for another ruling. As for if he reasons with a pure description, this qalb does not apply, because the description cannot become a ruling in any way, nor can the established ruling become a cause for it, because it precedes the ruling. 'With two handfuls of it' except for one who makes riba in large quantity prohibited in the sale of that which is sold by measure, necessitating the occurrence of riba in small quantity like precious metals, for riba occurs in both small and large, and the commonality is that riba occurs in both. 'Rather the occurrence of riba etc.' i.e., we have inverted the opponent's causal reasoning and made its occurrence in large quantity a ruling, which was a cause in the opponent's qiyas, and made its occurrence in small quantity a cause, which was a ruling in his qiyas. In it is that qalb only occurs in the original, and the author has performed qalb on the branch, and it is answered that qalb on the branch necessitates qalb on the original. 'The one seeking refuge in the Haram': He is one who owes retaliation for life. If he seeks refuge in the Haram, he is not killed there according to us, due to His statement: 'And whoever enters it is secure' (Quran 3:97), except that he is not fed, given drink, sat with, or sold to until he is forced to leave, then he is killed outside the Haram. According to al-Shafi'i, he is killed in the Haram by qiyas to one who owes retaliation for a limb, because if he seeks refuge in the Haram, retaliation is taken from him by agreement. The commonality is that each is a case of retaliation. Al-Shafi'i's followers said: The prohibition of taking life necessitates the prohibition of taking a limb, meaning that the prohibition of taking life is a cause for the prohibition of taking a limb, like the game of the Haram; because = so also
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage defines the objection of qalb (inversion) in qiyas. It first describes two types: (1) swapping cause and effect, and (2) linguistically turning something upside down. The legal example given is from riba: the opponent argues that riba in large quantities causes riba in small quantities, but the Hanafi inverts this, claiming the opposite. The second example is about a murderer seeking refuge in the Haram; the Shafi'i argue that the prohibition of killing (cause) prohibits limb-cutting (effect), but the Hanafi invert the causality. The text also clarifies that qalb only applies when the cause is a ruling (hukm), not a pure description (wasf). It concludes by noting that the author's qalb on the branch implies a qalb on the original. The passage is dense with usul terminology and relies on works like al-Ma'dan.

Key Terms
القلب (al-qalb): Inversion; a type of objection in qiyas where the relationship between cause and effect is reversed.
المعلل (al-mu'allil): The one who provides the causal reasoning (ta'lil) in a qiyas.
المعلول (al-ma'lul): The effect or ruling that results from the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the objection of qalb valid?
A) When the cause is a pure description.
B) When the cause is a ruling (hukm).
C) In all cases of qiyas.
D) Only in cases of riba.

Key Takeaway
Qalb is a valid objection in qiyas only when the cause is a ruling (hukm), not a pure description, and it works by reversing the causal relationship.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
beginner
7 min
The Linguistic and Conceptual Foundation of Qalb (Inversion) in Qiyas
Defines qalb linguistically and introduces its two types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define qalb linguistically and legally, and distinguish its two general meanings as an objection in qiyas.

Core Idea
Qalb is an objection that changes the proof's structure to invalidate or reverse the opponent's reasoning.

Before diving into the mechanics of qalb, we need to understand what it means both linguistically and as a term in usul al-fiqh. This lesson explores the two senses of qalb in Arabic language—turning over and turning inside out—and how they map onto its use as a critique of analogical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic meanings of qalb
Qalb literally means to turn something over or inside out. Identify the two specific linguistic uses: flipping a bowl (top to bottom) and turning a bag inside out (inner to outer).
Both involve changing the original state of the object.
2
Recognize qalb as a technical term in usul
In usul al-fiqh, qalb is an objection (su'al) against qiyas where the objector changes the structure of the proponent's evidence by inverting the relationship between cause and effect or using the same cause to prove the opposite ruling.
This is not arbitrary; it follows specific conditions.
3
Identify the two types of qalb in qiyas
The passage states that qalb is of two types: (1) making the cause into an effect, and (2) using the cause to prove the opposite ruling. Both aim to invalidate or reverse the opponent's argument.
These correspond to the linguistic meanings of inversion.
4
Connect the linguistic and technical meanings
Just as linguistic qalb changes the shape of a physical object, technical qalb changes the configuration of a proof, turning what was evidence for the opponent into evidence against him.
This analogy is explicitly drawn in the text.

البحث الرابع في القياس فصل في الأسئلة المتوجهة على القياس وأما القلب فنوعان: أحدهما: أن يجعل ما جعله المعلل علة للحكم معلولاً لذلك الحكم. ومثاله في الشرعيات: جريان الربا في الكثير يوجب جريانه في القليل كالأثمان، هذا النوع من القلب الحفنة فيحرم بيع من الطعام بالحفنتين منه. من الطعام مما يكال قلنا: لا بل جريان الربا في القليل يوجب جريانه في الكثير كالأثمان. أي ليس الأمر كذلك وكذلك في مسألة الملتجىء بالحرم: حرمة إتلاف النفس يوجب حرمة إتلاف الطرف وأما القلب: هو في اللغة يستعمل في معنيين: أحدهما: أن يجعل أسفل الشيء أعلاه وأعلاه أسفله كقلب القصعة والكوز. وثانيهما: أن يجعل باطن الشيء ظاهره وظاهره باطنه كقلب الجراب والثوب، وكلاهما يرجعان إلى شيء واحد وهو تغيير هيأة الشيء على خلاف الهيأة التي كان عليها، فكذا في القياس يستعمل القلب الصحيح بمعنيين، وكلاهما يرجعان إلى معنى واحد وهو تغيير الدليل إلى هيأة تخالف التي كان عليها كذا قيل. معلولاً لذلك إلخ: يعني ما جعله المعلل علة جعله السائل معلولاً أي حكماً، وما جعله المعلل جعله السائل علة، وفيه إبطال التعليل بإبطال علته بجعلها حكماً، وإنما يصح هذا النوع من القلب فيما إذا علل المستدل بالحكم بأن جعل ما كان حكماً في الأصل علة لحكم آخر فأما لو علل بالوصف المحض لا يرد عليه هذا القلب؛ لأن الوصف لا يصير حكماً بوجه ولا يصير الحكم الثابت علة له؛ لأنه سابق على الحكم كذا في "المعدن". بالحفنتين منه إلا من جعل الربا في الكثير حراماً في بيع ما يدخل تحت الكيل يوجب جريان الربا في القليل كالأثمان، فإنه يجري فيه الربا في القليل والكثير والجامع أن كلا منهما يجري فيه الربا. بل جريان الربا إلخ أي فقد قلبنا تعليل الخصم وجعلنا جريانه في الكثير حكما وهو علة في قياس الخصم، وجعلنا جريانه في القليل علة وهو حكم في قياسه وفيه أن القلب إنما يكون في الأصل والمصنف له قلب في الفرع، ويجاب بأن القلب في الفرع يستلزم القلب في الأصل كذا في "المعدن". الملتجيء بالحرم: وهو من عليه القصاص في النفس، فإنه إذا التجى إلى الحرم لا يقتل فيه عندنا؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِنًا﴾ الآية، إلا أنه لا يطعم ولا يسقى ولا يجالس ولا يباع حتى يضطر إلى الخروج، فيقتل خارج الحرم، وعند الشافعي له: يقتل في الحرم بالقياس على من عليه القصاص في الطرف؛ لأنه إذا التجى إلى الحرم يستوفي منه القصاص اتفاقاً، عليه القصاص في النفس والجامع أن كلاً منهما جان قال أصحاب الشافعي له حرمة إتلاف النفس يوجب حرمة إتلاف الطرف يعني أن حرمة إتلاف النفس علة لحرمة إتلاف الطرف كصيد الحرم؛ لأن = فكذا من كالصيد قلنا: بل حرمة إتلاف الطرف يوجب حرمة إتلاف النفس كالصيد، فإذا أي كصيدة الحرام جعلت علته معلولة لذلك الحكم لا تبقى علة له؛ لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء الواحد علة للشيء ومعلولاً له. الحكم والنوع الثاني من القلب: أن يجعل السائل ما جعله المعلل علة لما ادعاه من الحكم علة لضد ذلك الحكم فيصير حجة للسائل بعد أن كان حجة للمعلل. مثاله: صوم رمضان صوم فرض، فيشترط التعيين له كالقضاء. صوم رمضان قلنا: لما كان الصوم فرضاً لا يشترط التعيين له بعد ما تعين اليوم له كالقضاء. لأنه تحصيل الحاصل حرمة نفسه يوجب حرمة طرفه، وحرمة الطرف غير ثابت في الملتجيء بالإجماع، فعلم أن حرمة النفس أيضاً ثابت، وإلا يلزم تخلف الحكم عن العلة وهو باطل كذا في بعض الحواشي. والنوع الثاني إلخ: قلب الوصف أي العلة شاهداً على المعلل بعد أن كان شاهداً له. [الشافي: ص ۲۸۰] غير بعد أن كان حجة إلخ: أي ذلك الوصف حجة للمعلل وشاهد له، وهذا كقلب الجراب وهو جعل ظهره بطناً وبطنه ظهراً فأي الوصف كان شاهداً عليك قلبته تجعله شاهداً لك وكان ظهره إليك، فصار وجهه إليك، وهذا النوع من القلب لا يتحقق إلا بوصف زائد فيه تفسير الوصف الأول لا مبدل له؛ لأن الوصف الواحد لا يمكن أن يكون شاهداً لحكمين متنافيين بدون الزيادة كذا في "المعدن". لما كان الصوم إلخ قلت: ليس هذا جعل دليل المعلل وعلته دليلاً لنا، وعلة لحكمنا بعدم التعيين، بل بعد ضم أمر زائد إليه هو تعين اليوم في الصوم وهذا ليس عكساً حقيقياً بل صورياً، ثم اعلم أن كل هذه المقاولات وإنحاء النظر والمحاورات مبنية على الغفلة ومطارحه قبل تنقيح محل البحث وتحقيق مضمون المدعي، فإنه ينقح أولاً أن الحكم المعلل بالفرضية هل هو وجوب مطلق التعيين فمسلم وغير ضار؛ لأنه تعيين شرعي أو وجوب خصوص التعيين العبدي فضار، لكنه غير مسلم اقتضاء العلة له، فبعد التنقيح لا يبقى للسائل إلا مقام المنع أو النقض أو المعارضة كما حققه أهل المناظرة كذا في "الحصول". بعد ما تعين إلخ وهذا وصف زائد؛ لأن فيه تعيينه للوصف الأول؛ لأن كلامنا فيه لا في نفس الفرض كالقضاء أي كصوم القضاء، ولكن صوم بعد الشروع فيه، وصوم رمضان يتعين فيه؛ لأنه نفي سائر الصيامات. القضاء يتعين
The fourth investigation on analogy. Chapter on objections directed at analogy. As for inversion (qalb), it is of two types: First: that the objector makes what the proponent made a cause for the ruling into an effect of that ruling. Its example in legal matters: the flow of riba in a large quantity necessitates its flow in a small quantity like precious metals. This type of inversion: a handful, so selling food with two handfuls of it is prohibited. From food that is measured. We said: No, rather the flow of riba in a small quantity necessitates its flow in a large quantity like precious metals. That is, it is not as such. Similarly in the issue of the one seeking refuge in the Sanctuary: the inviolability of taking life necessitates the inviolability of taking a limb. As for inversion (qalb) linguistically, it is used in two meanings: first: making the top of something its bottom and its bottom its top, like turning over a bowl or a jug. Second: making the inside of something its outside and its outside its inside, like turning a bag or a garment inside out. Both revert to one thing, which is changing the state of something contrary to the state it was on. Thus, in analogy, sound inversion is used in two meanings, both reverting to one meaning: changing the proof into a state contrary to that which it was on, as was said. 'Made an effect for that' etc.: meaning what the proponent made a cause, the objector made an effect, i.e., a ruling, and what the proponent made a ruling, the objector made a cause. In it is invalidation of the reasoning by invalidating its cause by making it a ruling. This type of inversion is only valid when the proponent reasons with the ruling by making what was a ruling in the original a cause for another ruling. As for if he reasons with a pure description, this inversion does not apply to him, because a description cannot become a ruling in any way, nor can an established ruling become a cause for it, because it precedes the ruling. As in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'With two handfuls of it' except that he who makes riba in large quantity prohibited in selling what is measured necessitates the flow of riba in small quantity like precious metals, then riba flows in both small and large, and the commonality is that riba flows in each. 'Rather the flow of riba' etc., meaning we have inverted the opponent's reasoning and made its flow in large quantity a ruling, while it was a cause in the opponent's analogy, and we made its flow in small quantity a cause, while it was a ruling in his analogy. In it is that inversion is only in the original, and the author made an inversion in the branch. It is answered that inversion in the branch entails inversion in the original, as in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'The one seeking refuge in the Sanctuary': he is one who is subject to retaliation for life. If he seeks refuge in the Sanctuary, he is not killed there according to us, due to His saying, exalted: 'And whoever enters it is safe,' the verse, but he is not fed, given drink, socialized with, or sold to until he is forced to exit, then he is killed outside the Sanctuary. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, he is killed in the Sanctuary by analogy to one subject to retaliation for a limb, because if he seeks refuge in the Sanctuary, retaliation is exacted from him by agreement, one subject to retaliation for life. The commonality is that each is a life-taker. The companions of al-Shafi'i said: the inviolability of taking life necessitates the inviolability of taking a limb, meaning that the inviolability of taking life is a cause for the inviolability of taking a limb, like the game of the Sanctuary, because... So similarly from... Like the game. We said: rather the inviolability of taking a limb necessitates the inviolability of taking life, like the game, then if it is made an effect for that ruling, it does not remain a cause for it, because it is impossible for one thing to be both a cause and an effect for something. The ruling. The second type of inversion: that the objector makes what the proponent made a cause for the ruling he claimed into a cause for the opposite of that ruling, so it becomes a proof for the objector after it was a proof for the proponent. Its example: fasting Ramadan is a prescribed fast, so specification is required for it like make-up fasting. Fasting Ramadan. We said: since the fast is prescribed, specification is not required for it after the day is specified for it, like make-up fasting. Because it is obtaining what is already obtained. The inviolability of himself necessitates the inviolability of his limb, and the inviolability of the limb is not established for the one seeking refuge by consensus, so it is known that the inviolability of the self is also established; otherwise, the effect lagging behind the cause would occur, which is invalid. As in some glosses. 'The second type' etc.: inversion of the description, i.e., the cause as a witness against the proponent after it was a witness for him. [al-Shafi: p. 280] 'Other than after it was a proof' etc.: meaning that description was a proof for the proponent and a witness for him. This is like turning a bag inside out, making its back its front and its front its back. So whichever description was a witness against you, you turn it to make it a witness for you. It was facing away from you, so it faces you. This type of inversion is only realized with an extra description that interprets the first description, not replacing it, because a single description cannot be a witness for two contradictory rulings without an addition, as in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'Since the fast' etc. I said: this is not making the proponent's proof and cause a proof for us and a cause for our ruling of non-specification, but rather after attaching an additional matter to it, which is the specification of the day in the fast, and this is not a true inversion but a formal one. Then know that all these discussions, modes of consideration, and debates are based on heedlessness and contention before refining the subject of investigation and verifying the content of the claim. For it is first refined: whether the ruling reasoned with prescription is an absolute obligation of specification, which is conceded and harmless because it is a legal specification, or an obligation of particular servant specification, which is harmful, but it is not conceded that the cause necessitates it. After refinement, the objector has no position except denial, invalidation, or counterargument, as the masters of disputation have verified, as in 'al-Husul'. 'After the day is specified' etc., and this is an extra description because it contains the specification of the first description; because our discussion is about it, not about the prescription itself. Like make-up fasting, i.e., like the fast of make-up, but the fast after beginning it, and the fast of Ramadan is specified for it because it negates all other fasts. Make-up fasting is specified.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on objections (as'ilah) against qiyas (analogical reasoning). It discusses a specific type of objection called 'qalb' (inversion). The author first defines qalb linguistically and then divides it into two types as used in legal reasoning. The first type of qalb involves the objector taking the cause ('illa) that the proponent used for a ruling and instead making it the effect (ma'lul) of that same ruling, thereby inverting the relationship. The example given is from riba (usury/interest): if the proponent argues that because riba flows in large quantities of food, it must flow in small quantities as well (similar to currency), the objector inverts this by saying rather the flow in small quantities necessitates flow in large ones. This inversion invalidates the proponent's reasoning by showing that the causal relationship is not fixed. The commentary notes that this type of qalb only works if the proponent's cause is itself a ruling (hukm), not a pure description (wasf). The second example involves a murderer seeking refuge in the Haram (Sanctuary). The Hanafi position is that the murderer is not executed inside the Haram due to the verse 'And whoever enters it is safe'; he is instead pressured to leave and then executed outside. The Shafi'i position uses qiyas: since a thief's hand is amputated inside the Haram, so too a murderer should be executed inside. The Hanafi response inverts the Shafi'i cause: instead of the inviolability of life (hurmah al-nafs) being the cause for the inviolability of a limb (as in hunting), here the inviolability of the limb (which is not established by consensus for the sanctuary-seeker) would necessitate the inviolability of life, but since the limb inviolability is not proven, the inversion shows the flaw. The second type of qalb involves the objector taking the proponent's cause and using it to prove the opposite ruling, thus turning the proponent's evidence against him. The example given is fasting Ramadan: the proponent says it is a prescribed fast, therefore it requires specification (niyyah ta'yiniyyah) like make-up fasts. The Hanafi response: since the day is already specified for Ramadan (by the calendar), no additional specification is needed; the cause (prescription) does not necessitate specification because the day is already designated. The commentary explains that this type requires an extra description (wasf za'id) to change the direction of the proof. The passage ends with a methodological note that these dialectical exchanges rely on proper refinement of the issue before debate; otherwise, the objector's valid responses are only denial (man'), invalidation (naqd), or counterargument (mu'aradah). Throughout, the Hanafi school's positions are represented as the sound ones, with references to authoritative works like 'al-Ma'dan'. This is a usul discussion of a specific objection technique, not a direct fatwa, but it illustrates how Hanafi jurists defend their rulings through qiyas and its refutations.

Key Terms
القلب (al-qalb): In usul al-fiqh, a type of objection (su'al) in qiyas where the objector inverts the relationship between cause and effect or uses the opponent's cause to prove the opposite ruling.
المعلل (al-mu'allil): The proponent who presents a qiyas argument by identifying a cause ('illa) for a ruling.
السائل (al-sa'il): The objector who raises an objection (su'al) against the qiyas, in this case by applying qalb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the single conceptual commonality between the two linguistic meanings of qalb?
A) Both involve a change in ownership
B) Both involve changing the configuration of an object
C) Both involve aesthetic transformation
D) Both involve repetition of an action

Key Takeaway
Qalb is a structured objection in qiyas that systematically alters the proof's configuration, mirroring its linguistic meanings of inversion.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
First Type of Qalb: Inversion of Cause and Effect (Qalb al-Ma'lul 'ala al-'Illah)
Explains the first type of qalb with conditions and examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the mechanism of the first type of qalb, identify its condition (cause must be a ruling), and apply it to the riba and Haram examples to invalidate a qiyas.

Core Idea
Type 1 qalb swaps cause and effect to break the qiyas, but only works when the cause is a ruling, not a pure description.

The first type of qalb is a powerful objection: the objector inverts the causal relationship the proponent established, making the effect into the cause and vice versa. This effectively invalidates the proponent's reasoning by showing the inconsistency of treating a ruling as both cause and effect. In this lesson, we explore the condition that makes this type valid—the cause must be a ruling (hukm)—and see it applied in two classic Hanafi examples: the riba analogy and the case of a murderer seeking refuge in the Haram.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mechanism of type 1 qalb
The objector takes the proponent's cause and makes it the effect (ruling), and takes the effect and makes it the cause. This proves that the causal link is not fixed and can be reversed, invalidating the qiyas.
This only works if the cause itself was a ruling, not a description.
2
Identify the condition: cause must be a ruling (hukm)
If the proponent's cause is a pure description (wasf) like 'redness' or 'measurement', it cannot serve as an effect (ruling) because descriptions are not rulings. The condition ensures the inversion is logically possible.
The passage explicitly says this type is only valid when the proponent reasoned with a ruling as the cause.
3
Apply to the riba example
The proponent argues: riba flow in large quantity → causes riba flow in small quantity (like currency). The objector inverts: riba flow in small quantity → causes riba flow in large quantity. Thus the causal direction is symmetrical, so the specific claim fails.
Notice the cause here is 'flow of riba in large quantity', which is a ruling (hukm), meeting the condition.
4
Apply to the Haram sanctuary example
The Shafi'i opponent analogizes: since a thief's hand is amputated inside the Haram, similarly a murderer should be executed inside. The cause is the inviolability of life (hurmah al-nafs) leading to inviolability of limbs. The Hanafi objector inverts: inviolability of limbs → inviolability of life. Since inviolability of limbs is not established for the murderer (by consensus, amputation occurs inside), the inversion shows the cause is not sound.
This example also involves a dispute between Hanafi and Shafi'i, illustrating qalb in inter-madhhab debate.

الأصل والمصنف له قلب في الفرع، ويجاب بأن القلب في الفرع يستلزم القلب في الأصل كذا في "المعدن". الملتجيء بالحرم: وهو من عليه القصاص في النفس، فإنه إذا التجى إلى الحرم لا يقتل فيه عندنا؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِنًا﴾ الآية، إلا أنه لا يطعم ولا يسقى ولا يجالس ولا يباع حتى يضطر إلى الخروج، فيقتل خارج الحرم، وعند الشافعي له: يقتل في الحرم بالقياس على من عليه القصاص في الطرف؛ لأنه إذا التجى إلى الحرم يستوفي منه القصاص اتفاقاً، عليه القصاص في النفس والجامع أن كلاً منهما جان قال أصحاب الشافعي له حرمة إتلاف النفس يوجب حرمة إتلاف الطرف يعني أن حرمة إتلاف النفس علة لحرمة إتلاف الطرف كصيد الحرم؛ لأن = فكذا من كالصيد قلنا: بل حرمة إتلاف الطرف يوجب حرمة إتلاف النفس كالصيد، فإذا أي كصيدة الحرام جعلت علته معلولة لذلك الحكم لا تبقى علة له؛ لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء الواحد علة للشيء ومعلولاً له. الحكم والنوع الثاني من القلب: أن يجعل السائل ما جعله المعلل علة لما ادعاه من الحكم علة لضد ذلك الحكم فيصير حجة للسائل بعد أن كان حجة للمعلل. مثاله: صوم رمضان صوم فرض، فيشترط التعيين له كالقضاء. صوم رمضان قلنا: لما كان الصوم فرضاً لا يشترط التعيين له بعد ما تعين اليوم له كالقضاء. لأنه تحصيل الحاصل حرمة نفسه يوجب حرمة طرفه، وحرمة الطرف غير ثابت في الملتجيء بالإجماع، فعلم أن حرمة النفس أيضاً ثابت، وإلا يلزم تخلف الحكم عن العلة وهو باطل كذا في بعض الحواشي. والنوع الثاني إلخ: قلب الوصف أي العلة شاهداً على المعلل بعد أن كان شاهداً له. [الشافي: ص ۲۸۰] غير بعد أن كان حجة إلخ: أي ذلك الوصف حجة للمعلل وشاهد له، وهذا كقلب الجراب وهو جعل ظهره بطناً وبطنه ظهراً فأي الوصف كان شاهداً عليك قلبته تجعله شاهداً لك وكان ظهره إليك، فصار وجهه إليك، وهذا النوع من القلب لا يتحقق إلا بوصف زائد فيه تفسير الوصف الأول لا مبدل له؛ لأن الوصف الواحد لا يمكن أن يكون شاهداً لحكمين متنافيين بدون الزيادة كذا في "المعدن". لما كان الصوم إلخ قلت: ليس هذا جعل دليل المعلل وعلته دليلاً لنا، وعلة لحكمنا بعدم التعيين، بل بعد ضم أمر زائد إليه هو تعين اليوم في الصوم وهذا ليس عكساً حقيقياً بل صورياً، ثم اعلم أن كل هذه المقاولات وإنحاء النظر والمحاورات مبنية على الغفلة ومطارحه قبل تنقيح محل البحث وتحقيق مضمون المدعي، فإنه ينقح أولاً أن الحكم المعلل بالفرضية هل هو وجوب مطلق التعيين فمسلم وغير ضار؛ لأنه تعيين شرعي أو وجوب خصوص التعيين العبدي فضار، لكنه غير مسلم اقتضاء العلة له، فبعد التنقيح لا يبقى للسائل إلا مقام المنع أو النقض أو المعارضة كما حققه أهل المناظرة كذا في "الحصول". بعد ما تعين إلخ وهذا وصف زائد؛ لأن فيه تعيينه للوصف الأول؛ لأن كلامنا فيه لا في نفس الفرض كالقضاء أي كصوم القضاء، ولكن صوم بعد الشروع فيه، وصوم رمضان يتعين فيه؛ لأنه نفي سائر الصيامات. القضاء يتعين
The fourth investigation on analogy. Chapter on objections directed at analogy. As for inversion (qalb), it is of two types: First: that the objector makes what the proponent made a cause for the ruling into an effect of that ruling. Its example in legal matters: the flow of riba in a large quantity necessitates its flow in a small quantity like precious metals. This type of inversion: a handful, so selling food with two handfuls of it is prohibited. From food that is measured. We said: No, rather the flow of riba in a small quantity necessitates its flow in a large quantity like precious metals. That is, it is not as such. Similarly in the issue of the one seeking refuge in the Sanctuary: the inviolability of taking life necessitates the inviolability of taking a limb. As for inversion (qalb) linguistically, it is used in two meanings: first: making the top of something its bottom and its bottom its top, like turning over a bowl or a jug. Second: making the inside of something its outside and its outside its inside, like turning a bag or a garment inside out. Both revert to one thing, which is changing the state of something contrary to the state it was on. Thus, in analogy, sound inversion is used in two meanings, both reverting to one meaning: changing the proof into a state contrary to that which it was on, as was said. 'Made an effect for that' etc.: meaning what the proponent made a cause, the objector made an effect, i.e., a ruling, and what the proponent made a ruling, the objector made a cause. In it is invalidation of the reasoning by invalidating its cause by making it a ruling. This type of inversion is only valid when the proponent reasons with the ruling by making what was a ruling in the original a cause for another ruling. As for if he reasons with a pure description, this inversion does not apply to him, because a description cannot become a ruling in any way, nor can an established ruling become a cause for it, because it precedes the ruling. As in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'With two handfuls of it' except that he who makes riba in large quantity prohibited in selling what is measured necessitates the flow of riba in small quantity like precious metals, then riba flows in both small and large, and the commonality is that riba flows in each. 'Rather the flow of riba' etc., meaning we have inverted the opponent's reasoning and made its flow in large quantity a ruling, while it was a cause in the opponent's analogy, and we made its flow in small quantity a cause, while it was a ruling in his analogy. In it is that inversion is only in the original, and the author made an inversion in the branch. It is answered that inversion in the branch entails inversion in the original, as in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'The one seeking refuge in the Sanctuary': he is one who is subject to retaliation for life. If he seeks refuge in the Sanctuary, he is not killed there according to us, due to His saying, exalted: 'And whoever enters it is safe,' the verse, but he is not fed, given drink, socialized with, or sold to until he is forced to exit, then he is killed outside the Sanctuary. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, he is killed in the Sanctuary by analogy to one subject to retaliation for a limb, because if he seeks refuge in the Sanctuary, retaliation is exacted from him by agreement, one subject to retaliation for life. The commonality is that each is a life-taker. The companions of al-Shafi'i said: the inviolability of taking life necessitates the inviolability of taking a limb, meaning that the inviolability of taking life is a cause for the inviolability of taking a limb, like the game of the Sanctuary, because... So similarly from... Like the game. We said: rather the inviolability of taking a limb necessitates the inviolability of taking life, like the game, then if it is made an effect for that ruling, it does not remain a cause for it, because it is impossible for one thing to be both a cause and an effect for something. The ruling. The second type of inversion: that the objector makes what the proponent made a cause for the ruling he claimed into a cause for the opposite of that ruling, so it becomes a proof for the objector after it was a proof for the proponent. Its example: fasting Ramadan is a prescribed fast, so specification is required for it like make-up fasting. Fasting Ramadan. We said: since the fast is prescribed, specification is not required for it after the day is specified for it, like make-up fasting. Because it is obtaining what is already obtained. The inviolability of himself necessitates the inviolability of his limb, and the inviolability of the limb is not established for the one seeking refuge by consensus, so it is known that the inviolability of the self is also established; otherwise, the effect lagging behind the cause would occur, which is invalid. As in some glosses. 'The second type' etc.: inversion of the description, i.e., the cause as a witness against the proponent after it was a witness for him. [al-Shafi: p. 280] 'Other than after it was a proof' etc.: meaning that description was a proof for the proponent and a witness for him. This is like turning a bag inside out, making its back its front and its front its back. So whichever description was a witness against you, you turn it to make it a witness for you. It was facing away from you, so it faces you. This type of inversion is only realized with an extra description that interprets the first description, not replacing it, because a single description cannot be a witness for two contradictory rulings without an addition, as in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'Since the fast' etc. I said: this is not making the proponent's proof and cause a proof for us and a cause for our ruling of non-specification, but rather after attaching an additional matter to it, which is the specification of the day in the fast, and this is not a true inversion but a formal one. Then know that all these discussions, modes of consideration, and debates are based on heedlessness and contention before refining the subject of investigation and verifying the content of the claim. For it is first refined: whether the ruling reasoned with prescription is an absolute obligation of specification, which is conceded and harmless because it is a legal specification, or an obligation of particular servant specification, which is harmful, but it is not conceded that the cause necessitates it. After refinement, the objector has no position except denial, invalidation, or counterargument, as the masters of disputation have verified, as in 'al-Husul'. 'After the day is specified' etc., and this is an extra description because it contains the specification of the first description; because our discussion is about it, not about the prescription itself. Like make-up fasting, i.e., like the fast of make-up, but the fast after beginning it, and the fast of Ramadan is specified for it because it negates all other fasts. Make-up fasting is specified.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on objections (as'ilah) against qiyas (analogical reasoning). It discusses a specific type of objection called 'qalb' (inversion). The author first defines qalb linguistically and then divides it into two types as used in legal reasoning. The first type of qalb involves the objector taking the cause ('illa) that the proponent used for a ruling and instead making it the effect (ma'lul) of that same ruling, thereby inverting the relationship. The example given is from riba (usury/interest): if the proponent argues that because riba flows in large quantities of food, it must flow in small quantities as well (similar to currency), the objector inverts this by saying rather the flow in small quantities necessitates flow in large ones. This inversion invalidates the proponent's reasoning by showing that the causal relationship is not fixed. The commentary notes that this type of qalb only works if the proponent's cause is itself a ruling (hukm), not a pure description (wasf). The second example involves a murderer seeking refuge in the Haram (Sanctuary). The Hanafi position is that the murderer is not executed inside the Haram due to the verse 'And whoever enters it is safe'; he is instead pressured to leave and then executed outside. The Shafi'i position uses qiyas: since a thief's hand is amputated inside the Haram, so too a murderer should be executed inside. The Hanafi response inverts the Shafi'i cause: instead of the inviolability of life (hurmah al-nafs) being the cause for the inviolability of a limb (as in hunting), here the inviolability of the limb (which is not established by consensus for the sanctuary-seeker) would necessitate the inviolability of life, but since the limb inviolability is not proven, the inversion shows the flaw. The second type of qalb involves the objector taking the proponent's cause and using it to prove the opposite ruling, thus turning the proponent's evidence against him. The example given is fasting Ramadan: the proponent says it is a prescribed fast, therefore it requires specification (niyyah ta'yiniyyah) like make-up fasts. The Hanafi response: since the day is already specified for Ramadan (by the calendar), no additional specification is needed; the cause (prescription) does not necessitate specification because the day is already designated. The commentary explains that this type requires an extra description (wasf za'id) to change the direction of the proof. The passage ends with a methodological note that these dialectical exchanges rely on proper refinement of the issue before debate; otherwise, the objector's valid responses are only denial (man'), invalidation (naqd), or counterargument (mu'aradah). Throughout, the Hanafi school's positions are represented as the sound ones, with references to authoritative works like 'al-Ma'dan'. This is a usul discussion of a specific objection technique, not a direct fatwa, but it illustrates how Hanafi jurists defend their rulings through qiyas and its refutations.

Key Terms
القلب (al-qalb): First type: making the opponent's cause into an effect and vice versa.
الوصف المحض (al-wasf al-mahd): A pure description that is not a ruling; it cannot serve as a cause in type 1 qalb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key condition for the first type of qalb to be valid?
A) The objector must provide a textual proof.
B) The proponent's cause must be a ruling (hukm), not a description (wasf).
C) The proponent must be a Shafi'i.
D) The example must involve the Haram.

Key Takeaway
The first type of qalb inverts cause and effect to invalidate a qiyas, but only when the cause is a ruling—a condition that ensures logical coherence.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
Second Type of Qalb: Turning the Cause Against the Proponent (Qalb al-'Illah li al-Didd)
Explains the second type of qalb with the fasting Ramadan example and the need for an extra description

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the second type of qalb, identify the requirement of an extra description (wasf za'id), and apply it to the fasting Ramadan example.

Core Idea
Type 2 qalb uses the opponent's own cause to prove the opposite ruling, but only with an extra description that changes the proof's direction.

The second type of qalb is an elegant objection: the objector takes the very cause that the proponent used to support his ruling and turns it into evidence for the opposite ruling. This is not a mere reversal but a redirection of the same evidence. However, as the passage explains, this is only possible when there is an extra description (wasf za'id) that allows the cause to support a different conclusion. We explore this through the example of whether fasting Ramadan requires a specific intention (ta'yin) in the niyyah, similar to make-up fasts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the mechanism of type 2 qalb
The objector takes the proponent's cause and uses it to prove a ruling opposite to what the proponent claimed. The cause itself becomes a witness for the objector.
This is compared to turning a bag inside out: what was facing away now faces you.
2
Identify the need for an extra description (wasf za'id)
A single description cannot be a witness for two contradictory rulings. Therefore, a type 2 qalb requires an additional factor that allows the same description to support the opposite ruling.
The passage says this type is only realized with an extra description that interprets the first description.
3
Apply to the fasting example
The proponent argues: Fasting Ramadan is prescribed (cause) → it requires specification of intention (ruling), like make-up fasting. The objector: The same cause (prescribed) → no specification is needed because the day is already specified (extra description). The day is already determined for Ramadan (by calendar), so the cause does not necessitate specification.
The extra description is the pre-determination of the day for Ramadan. Without it, the inversion would not work.
4
Recognize the formal nature of this inversion
The commentary notes that this is not a true inversion but a formal one, because the objector adds a new element (the day's specification) rather than simply reversing the cause.
True qalb would be simply flipping, but here an extra element is needed.

البحث الرابع في القياس فصل في الأسئلة المتوجهة على القياس وأما القلب فنوعان: أحدهما: أن يجعل ما جعله المعلل علة للحكم معلولاً لذلك الحكم. ومثاله في الشرعيات: جريان الربا في الكثير يوجب جريانه في القليل كالأثمان، هذا النوع من القلب الحفنة فيحرم بيع من الطعام بالحفنتين منه. من الطعام مما يكال قلنا: لا بل جريان الربا في القليل يوجب جريانه في الكثير كالأثمان. أي ليس الأمر كذلك وكذلك في مسألة الملتجىء بالحرم: حرمة إتلاف النفس يوجب حرمة إتلاف الطرف وأما القلب: هو في اللغة يستعمل في معنيين: أحدهما: أن يجعل أسفل الشيء أعلاه وأعلاه أسفله كقلب القصعة والكوز. وثانيهما: أن يجعل باطن الشيء ظاهره وظاهره باطنه كقلب الجراب والثوب، وكلاهما يرجعان إلى شيء واحد وهو تغيير هيأة الشيء على خلاف الهيأة التي كان عليها، فكذا في القياس يستعمل القلب الصحيح بمعنيين، وكلاهما يرجعان إلى معنى واحد وهو تغيير الدليل إلى هيأة تخالف التي كان عليها كذا قيل. معلولاً لذلك إلخ: يعني ما جعله المعلل علة جعله السائل معلولاً أي حكماً، وما جعله المعلل جعله السائل علة، وفيه إبطال التعليل بإبطال علته بجعلها حكماً، وإنما يصح هذا النوع من القلب فيما إذا علل المستدل بالحكم بأن جعل ما كان حكماً في الأصل علة لحكم آخر فأما لو علل بالوصف المحض لا يرد عليه هذا القلب؛ لأن الوصف لا يصير حكماً بوجه ولا يصير الحكم الثابت علة له؛ لأنه سابق على الحكم كذا في "المعدن". بالحفنتين منه إلا من جعل الربا في الكثير حراماً في بيع ما يدخل تحت الكيل يوجب جريان الربا في القليل كالأثمان، فإنه يجري فيه الربا في القليل والكثير والجامع أن كلا منهما يجري فيه الربا. بل جريان الربا إلخ أي فقد قلبنا تعليل الخصم وجعلنا جريانه في الكثير حكما وهو علة في قياس الخصم، وجعلنا جريانه في القليل علة وهو حكم في قياسه وفيه أن القلب إنما يكون في الأصل والمصنف له قلب في الفرع، ويجاب بأن القلب في الفرع يستلزم القلب في الأصل كذا في "المعدن". الملتجيء بالحرم: وهو من عليه القصاص في النفس، فإنه إذا التجى إلى الحرم لا يقتل فيه عندنا؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ دَخَلَهُ كَانَ آمِنًا﴾ الآية، إلا أنه لا يطعم ولا يسقى ولا يجالس ولا يباع حتى يضطر إلى الخروج، فيقتل خارج الحرم، وعند الشافعي له: يقتل في الحرم بالقياس على من عليه القصاص في الطرف؛ لأنه إذا التجى إلى الحرم يستوفي منه القصاص اتفاقاً، عليه القصاص في النفس والجامع أن كلاً منهما جان قال أصحاب الشافعي له حرمة إتلاف النفس يوجب حرمة إتلاف الطرف يعني أن حرمة إتلاف النفس علة لحرمة إتلاف الطرف كصيد الحرم؛ لأن = فكذا من كالصيد قلنا: بل حرمة إتلاف الطرف يوجب حرمة إتلاف النفس كالصيد، فإذا أي كصيدة الحرام جعلت علته معلولة لذلك الحكم لا تبقى علة له؛ لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء الواحد علة للشيء ومعلولاً له. الحكم والنوع الثاني من القلب: أن يجعل السائل ما جعله المعلل علة لما ادعاه من الحكم علة لضد ذلك الحكم فيصير حجة للسائل بعد أن كان حجة للمعلل. مثاله: صوم رمضان صوم فرض، فيشترط التعيين له كالقضاء. صوم رمضان قلنا: لما كان الصوم فرضاً لا يشترط التعيين له بعد ما تعين اليوم له كالقضاء. لأنه تحصيل الحاصل حرمة نفسه يوجب حرمة طرفه، وحرمة الطرف غير ثابت في الملتجيء بالإجماع، فعلم أن حرمة النفس أيضاً ثابت، وإلا يلزم تخلف الحكم عن العلة وهو باطل كذا في بعض الحواشي. والنوع الثاني إلخ: قلب الوصف أي العلة شاهداً على المعلل بعد أن كان شاهداً له. [الشافي: ص ۲۸۰] غير بعد أن كان حجة إلخ: أي ذلك الوصف حجة للمعلل وشاهد له، وهذا كقلب الجراب وهو جعل ظهره بطناً وبطنه ظهراً فأي الوصف كان شاهداً عليك قلبته تجعله شاهداً لك وكان ظهره إليك، فصار وجهه إليك، وهذا النوع من القلب لا يتحقق إلا بوصف زائد فيه تفسير الوصف الأول لا مبدل له؛ لأن الوصف الواحد لا يمكن أن يكون شاهداً لحكمين متنافيين بدون الزيادة كذا في "المعدن". لما كان الصوم إلخ قلت: ليس هذا جعل دليل المعلل وعلته دليلاً لنا، وعلة لحكمنا بعدم التعيين، بل بعد ضم أمر زائد إليه هو تعين اليوم في الصوم وهذا ليس عكساً حقيقياً بل صورياً، ثم اعلم أن كل هذه المقاولات وإنحاء النظر والمحاورات مبنية على الغفلة ومطارحه قبل تنقيح محل البحث وتحقيق مضمون المدعي، فإنه ينقح أولاً أن الحكم المعلل بالفرضية هل هو وجوب مطلق التعيين فمسلم وغير ضار؛ لأنه تعيين شرعي أو وجوب خصوص التعيين العبدي فضار، لكنه غير مسلم اقتضاء العلة له، فبعد التنقيح لا يبقى للسائل إلا مقام المنع أو النقض أو المعارضة كما حققه أهل المناظرة كذا في "الحصول". بعد ما تعين إلخ وهذا وصف زائد؛ لأن فيه تعيينه للوصف الأول؛ لأن كلامنا فيه لا في نفس الفرض كالقضاء أي كصوم القضاء، ولكن صوم بعد الشروع فيه، وصوم رمضان يتعين فيه؛ لأنه نفي سائر الصيامات. القضاء يتعين
The fourth investigation on analogy. Chapter on objections directed at analogy. As for inversion (qalb), it is of two types: First: that the objector makes what the proponent made a cause for the ruling into an effect of that ruling. Its example in legal matters: the flow of riba in a large quantity necessitates its flow in a small quantity like precious metals. This type of inversion: a handful, so selling food with two handfuls of it is prohibited. From food that is measured. We said: No, rather the flow of riba in a small quantity necessitates its flow in a large quantity like precious metals. That is, it is not as such. Similarly in the issue of the one seeking refuge in the Sanctuary: the inviolability of taking life necessitates the inviolability of taking a limb. As for inversion (qalb) linguistically, it is used in two meanings: first: making the top of something its bottom and its bottom its top, like turning over a bowl or a jug. Second: making the inside of something its outside and its outside its inside, like turning a bag or a garment inside out. Both revert to one thing, which is changing the state of something contrary to the state it was on. Thus, in analogy, sound inversion is used in two meanings, both reverting to one meaning: changing the proof into a state contrary to that which it was on, as was said. 'Made an effect for that' etc.: meaning what the proponent made a cause, the objector made an effect, i.e., a ruling, and what the proponent made a ruling, the objector made a cause. In it is invalidation of the reasoning by invalidating its cause by making it a ruling. This type of inversion is only valid when the proponent reasons with the ruling by making what was a ruling in the original a cause for another ruling. As for if he reasons with a pure description, this inversion does not apply to him, because a description cannot become a ruling in any way, nor can an established ruling become a cause for it, because it precedes the ruling. As in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'With two handfuls of it' except that he who makes riba in large quantity prohibited in selling what is measured necessitates the flow of riba in small quantity like precious metals, then riba flows in both small and large, and the commonality is that riba flows in each. 'Rather the flow of riba' etc., meaning we have inverted the opponent's reasoning and made its flow in large quantity a ruling, while it was a cause in the opponent's analogy, and we made its flow in small quantity a cause, while it was a ruling in his analogy. In it is that inversion is only in the original, and the author made an inversion in the branch. It is answered that inversion in the branch entails inversion in the original, as in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'The one seeking refuge in the Sanctuary': he is one who is subject to retaliation for life. If he seeks refuge in the Sanctuary, he is not killed there according to us, due to His saying, exalted: 'And whoever enters it is safe,' the verse, but he is not fed, given drink, socialized with, or sold to until he is forced to exit, then he is killed outside the Sanctuary. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, he is killed in the Sanctuary by analogy to one subject to retaliation for a limb, because if he seeks refuge in the Sanctuary, retaliation is exacted from him by agreement, one subject to retaliation for life. The commonality is that each is a life-taker. The companions of al-Shafi'i said: the inviolability of taking life necessitates the inviolability of taking a limb, meaning that the inviolability of taking life is a cause for the inviolability of taking a limb, like the game of the Sanctuary, because... So similarly from... Like the game. We said: rather the inviolability of taking a limb necessitates the inviolability of taking life, like the game, then if it is made an effect for that ruling, it does not remain a cause for it, because it is impossible for one thing to be both a cause and an effect for something. The ruling. The second type of inversion: that the objector makes what the proponent made a cause for the ruling he claimed into a cause for the opposite of that ruling, so it becomes a proof for the objector after it was a proof for the proponent. Its example: fasting Ramadan is a prescribed fast, so specification is required for it like make-up fasting. Fasting Ramadan. We said: since the fast is prescribed, specification is not required for it after the day is specified for it, like make-up fasting. Because it is obtaining what is already obtained. The inviolability of himself necessitates the inviolability of his limb, and the inviolability of the limb is not established for the one seeking refuge by consensus, so it is known that the inviolability of the self is also established; otherwise, the effect lagging behind the cause would occur, which is invalid. As in some glosses. 'The second type' etc.: inversion of the description, i.e., the cause as a witness against the proponent after it was a witness for him. [al-Shafi: p. 280] 'Other than after it was a proof' etc.: meaning that description was a proof for the proponent and a witness for him. This is like turning a bag inside out, making its back its front and its front its back. So whichever description was a witness against you, you turn it to make it a witness for you. It was facing away from you, so it faces you. This type of inversion is only realized with an extra description that interprets the first description, not replacing it, because a single description cannot be a witness for two contradictory rulings without an addition, as in 'al-Ma'dan'. 'Since the fast' etc. I said: this is not making the proponent's proof and cause a proof for us and a cause for our ruling of non-specification, but rather after attaching an additional matter to it, which is the specification of the day in the fast, and this is not a true inversion but a formal one. Then know that all these discussions, modes of consideration, and debates are based on heedlessness and contention before refining the subject of investigation and verifying the content of the claim. For it is first refined: whether the ruling reasoned with prescription is an absolute obligation of specification, which is conceded and harmless because it is a legal specification, or an obligation of particular servant specification, which is harmful, but it is not conceded that the cause necessitates it. After refinement, the objector has no position except denial, invalidation, or counterargument, as the masters of disputation have verified, as in 'al-Husul'. 'After the day is specified' etc., and this is an extra description because it contains the specification of the first description; because our discussion is about it, not about the prescription itself. Like make-up fasting, i.e., like the fast of make-up, but the fast after beginning it, and the fast of Ramadan is specified for it because it negates all other fasts. Make-up fasting is specified.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on objections (as'ilah) against qiyas (analogical reasoning). It discusses a specific type of objection called 'qalb' (inversion). The author first defines qalb linguistically and then divides it into two types as used in legal reasoning. The first type of qalb involves the objector taking the cause ('illa) that the proponent used for a ruling and instead making it the effect (ma'lul) of that same ruling, thereby inverting the relationship. The example given is from riba (usury/interest): if the proponent argues that because riba flows in large quantities of food, it must flow in small quantities as well (similar to currency), the objector inverts this by saying rather the flow in small quantities necessitates flow in large ones. This inversion invalidates the proponent's reasoning by showing that the causal relationship is not fixed. The commentary notes that this type of qalb only works if the proponent's cause is itself a ruling (hukm), not a pure description (wasf). The second example involves a murderer seeking refuge in the Haram (Sanctuary). The Hanafi position is that the murderer is not executed inside the Haram due to the verse 'And whoever enters it is safe'; he is instead pressured to leave and then executed outside. The Shafi'i position uses qiyas: since a thief's hand is amputated inside the Haram, so too a murderer should be executed inside. The Hanafi response inverts the Shafi'i cause: instead of the inviolability of life (hurmah al-nafs) being the cause for the inviolability of a limb (as in hunting), here the inviolability of the limb (which is not established by consensus for the sanctuary-seeker) would necessitate the inviolability of life, but since the limb inviolability is not proven, the inversion shows the flaw. The second type of qalb involves the objector taking the proponent's cause and using it to prove the opposite ruling, thus turning the proponent's evidence against him. The example given is fasting Ramadan: the proponent says it is a prescribed fast, therefore it requires specification (niyyah ta'yiniyyah) like make-up fasts. The Hanafi response: since the day is already specified for Ramadan (by the calendar), no additional specification is needed; the cause (prescription) does not necessitate specification because the day is already designated. The commentary explains that this type requires an extra description (wasf za'id) to change the direction of the proof. The passage ends with a methodological note that these dialectical exchanges rely on proper refinement of the issue before debate; otherwise, the objector's valid responses are only denial (man'), invalidation (naqd), or counterargument (mu'aradah). Throughout, the Hanafi school's positions are represented as the sound ones, with references to authoritative works like 'al-Ma'dan'. This is a usul discussion of a specific objection technique, not a direct fatwa, but it illustrates how Hanafi jurists defend their rulings through qiyas and its refutations.

Key Terms
النوع الثاني من القلب (al-naw' al-thani min al-qalb): The objector uses the same cause to prove the opposite ruling, requiring an extra description.
الوصف الزائد (al-wasf al-za'id): An extra description that allows the cause to support a different ruling; it interprets the first description without replacing it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the essential requirement for the second type of qalb to be valid?
A) The cause must be a ruling (hukm).
B) There must be an extra description (wasf za'id).
C) The proponent must agree with the inversion.
D) The example must involve fasting.

Key Takeaway
The second type of qalb turns the proponent's cause into evidence for the opposite ruling but relies on an extra description to make the inversion logically sound.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Types of Objections to Qiyas: Qalb, 'Aks, and Fasad al-Wad'
Defines three types of objections in qiyas: Qalb, 'Aks, Fasad al-Wad'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the three types of objections to qiyas (Qalb, 'Aks, Fasad al-Wad') and apply them to analogical arguments in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Three objections dismantle qiyas: Qalb flips the cause, 'Aks forces a distinction, Fasad al-Wad attacks the cause's suitability.

This passage from Al-Shashi's Usul introduces three critical tools used in jurisprudential debate to challenge analogical reasoning. Each objection targets a different aspect of the qiyas: the cause itself (Qalb), the relationship between original and branch ('Aks), or the inherent appropriateness of the cause (Fasad al-Wad'). The text not only defines these but illustrates them with fiqh examples, showing how the Hanafi school defends its positions.

Guided Path
1
Classify each objection
Identify Qalb, 'Aks, and Fasad al-Wad' and their definitions. Note the key features that distinguish them.
Qalb involves reversing the cause; 'Aks forces a distinction; Fasad al-Wad attacks the cause's suitability.
2
Examine the mechanism
For each objection, understand how the questioner uses the proponent's own argument against them. Qalb requires an additional attribute; 'Aks uses the original case; Fasad al-Wad shows the cause is inappropriate.
3
Work through the examples
Analyze the given examples: the fast of Ramadan for Qalb, jewelry for 'Aks, and conversion of spouses for Fasad al-Wad'. See how the Hanafi response refutes each objection.
Focus on the role of the additional attribute in Qalb and the forced distinction in 'Aks.
4
Identify the Hanafi principle
Note the Hanafi positions: Islam preserves rights (against Fasad al-Wad), Ramadan fast does not require explicit intention (against Qalb), and men's jewelry is forbidden (against 'Aks).
5
Assess the conditions for validity
For Qalb, note that it is only valid with an additional attribute (wasf za'id). For 'Aks, it forces the proponent to find a difference. For Fasad al-Wad, it reveals that the cause is not suitable.
Without these conditions, the objections are invalid.

The fourth research: on Qiyas. Chapter on the questions directed against Qiyas. ... And the second type of Qalb is that the questioner makes what the proponent made as a cause for his claimed ruling a cause for the opposite of that ruling, so it becomes a proof for the questioner after it was a proof for the proponent. Example: The fast of Ramadan is an obligatory fast, so specification (niyya) is required for it like the makeup fast. We said: Since the fast is obligatory, specification is not required after the day has been specified for it, like the makeup fast. ... As for 'Aks (reverse), it means that the questioner adheres to the proponent's original case in a way that forces the proponent to point out the difference between the original and the branch. Example: Jewelry is prepared for wearing, so zakat is not obligatory on it, like worn-out clothes. We said: If jewelry were like clothes, then zakat would not be obligatory on men's jewelry like worn-out clothes. And as for Fasad al-Wad' (corruption of premise), it means that the cause is made a description not appropriate for that ruling. Example: In their saying regarding conversion of one spouse: difference of religion occurred over marriage so it invalidates it. The Shafi'is compare it to apostasy of one spouse, for they made Islam a cause for the removal of marital authority. We said: Islam is known to be a preserver of authority, so it cannot be effective in removing authority. Similarly in the issue of the free man's capability: he is free and able to marry, so it is not permissible for him to marry a slave woman as if he had a free wife under him. And also the explanation of 'Aks in language and terminology.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is from the chapter on questions (as'ilah) directed against Qiyas in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It defines three types of objections: Qalb (inversion), 'Aks (reverse), and Fasad al-Wad' (corruption of premise). Each is a tool for the questioner (sā'il) to challenge the proponent's (mu'allil) analogical argument. The text first discusses the second type of Qalb, where the questioner takes the opponent's cause (illa) and uses it to argue for the opposite ruling. The example given is the fast of Ramadan versus makeup fast (qada'). The Hanafi position (قلنا) explains that since the day is already specified by the calendar, no additional intention (niyya) is required, contrary to the opponent's claim. The commentary elaborates that this inversion requires an additional attribute (wasf za'id) to be valid; otherwise, it is impossible for one description to be a proof for two contradictory rulings. Then 'Aks is defined: the questioner forces the proponent to distinguish between the original and the branch by using the proponent's own original case. The example is jewelry (hulli) and worn-out clothes (thiyāb al-badhla). The Hanafi response is that men's jewelry is forbidden to wear, so it cannot be considered for wearing like clothes. Finally, Fasad al-Wad' is when the proposed cause (illa) is inherently inappropriate for the ruling, such as claiming that Islam (i.e., conversion) destroys marital authority, whereas Islam is known to preserve rights. The text contrasts the Hanafi view with the Shafi'i view on this issue. Throughout, the passage emphasizes that these objections must be applied correctly and that proper investigation (tanqih) of the subject matter often resolves the dispute.

Key Terms
القلب (al-qalb): An objection in qiyas where the questioner uses the opponent's cause to argue for the opposite ruling, requiring an additional attribute to be valid
العكس (al-'aks): An objection where the questioner forces the proponent to point out a difference between the original and branch by adhering to the proponent's original case
فساد الوضع (fasād al-wad'): An objection where the proposed cause (illa) is inherently unsuitable for the ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the second type of Qalb (inversion) require to be valid?
A) A complete reversal of the original analogy
B) An additional attribute (wasf zā'id) that allows the cause to support the opposite ruling
C) The questioner must concede the original analogy
D) The cause must be ambiguous

Key Takeaway
Understanding Qalb, 'Aks, and Fasad al-Wad' equips the student of usul with critical tools to analyze and challenge analogical arguments in Hanafi jurisprudence.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Four Objections to Qiyas in Hanafi Usul
Explains four types of objections to analogical reasoning with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify, define, and apply the four standard objections to qiyas—al-‘aks, fasād al-waḍ‘, al-naqḍ, and al-mu‘āraḍah—in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
A valid objection to qiyas must expose a flaw in the illa itself, not merely disagree with the ruling.

This passage from al-Shāshī's Uṣūl introduces the four classical objections (as‘ilah) used to challenge analogical reasoning in Hanafi legal theory. Understanding these objections is essential for both constructing sound analogies and critiquing weak ones. The text defines each type and illustrates them with concrete examples, including the famous dispute over whether conversion to Islam by one spouse immediately dissolves the marriage.

Guided Path
1
Understand al-‘aks (the inverse)
Al-‘aks forces the proponent of the analogy to admit a distinction between the original case (aṣl) and the branch (far‘) by adhering to the proponent's own aṣl. The student should recognize that this objection does not deny the illa but shows inconsistency when the analogy is applied to a related case.
Example: If ornaments are like clothes for zakat, then men's ornaments would also be exempt, but they are prohibited to wear, so the analogy fails.
2
Understand fasād al-waḍ‘ (corruption of premise)
This objection attacks the suitability of the attribute chosen as the illa. The student must see that if the illa is inherently contradictory to the ruling (e.g., using a protective attribute like Islam to justify destruction of marital authority), the analogy is invalid.
Example: Hanafis reject the Shafi‘i claim that Islam causes annulment because Islam is known to preserve rights (ʿāṣim).
3
Understand al-naqḍ (contradiction)
Al-naqḍ points out a case where the claimed illa exists but the ruling does not follow, revealing an inconsistency. The student must distinguish this from al-‘aks: naqḍ shows the illa is present but the ruling is absent, while al-‘aks forces a distinction.
Example: If the illa for zakat on ornaments is 'prepared for daily wear', then why is there no zakat on men's ornaments even though they are not for daily wear? (This is a hypothetical naqḍ.)
4
Understand al-mu‘āraḍah (counterargument)
Al-mu‘āraḍah involves bringing an alternative proof that negates the proponent's ruling. It is not a flaw in the illa but a competing evidence. The student must learn to differentiate mu‘āraḍah from naqḍ: mu‘āraḍah offers a separate proof, while naqḍ exposes inconsistency within the proponent's own reasoning.
Example: Instead of critiquing the illa, one might cite a hadith that directly rules on the case, overriding the qiyas.

Source Text

البحث الرابع في القياس
فصل في الأسئلة المتوجهة على القياس
وأمَّا العكس فتعني به أنْ يتمسك السائل بأصل المعلل على وجه يكون المعلّلُ مضطراً إلى وجه المفارقة بين الأصل والفرع.
ومثاله: الحلى أُعِدَّتْ للابتذال فلا يجب فيها الزكاة كثياب البذلة.
قلنا: لو كان الحلى بمنزلة الثياب فلا تجب الزكاة في حلي الرجال كثياب البذلة. وأما فسَادُ الوضع فالمراد به أَنْ يُجعل العلة وصفاً لا يليق بذلك الحكم.
مثاله: في قولهم في إسلام أحد الزوجين: اختلافُ الدِّين طرأ على النكاح فيُفسده، فساد الوضع. قال الشافعية: كارتداد أحد الزوجين، فإنه جَعَل الإسلام علة لزوال الملك. قلنا: الإسلام عُهدَ عاصماً للملك فلا يكون مؤثراً في زوال الملك.
وكذلك في مسألة طَوْل الحَرَّة: أنه حرّ قادرٌ على النكاح، فلا يجوز له الأمة كما لو كانت تحته حرة.
وأمَّا العكس وهو لغة أن يرد الشيء إلى السنة الأولى، وفي الإصطلاح أن يتمسك السائل بأصل المعلل أي: بما جعله المعلل أصلاً مقيساً عليه لضد ما فرع المعلل أي: لضد الحكم الذي جعله المعلل فرعاً، وهو الحكم المدعى بالقياس. كثياب البذلة: فاضطر المعلل في صحة قياسه إلى قبول الفرق بين الأصل والفرع أي: بين حلّي الرجال وثياب البذلة بأن يقال: حلّي الرجال حرام الاستعمال، فلم يتحقق فيه الابتذال بخلاف الثياب. في إسلام أحد إلخ: فإنهم قالوا في إسلام أحدهما: إنه تقع الفرقة بمجرد الإسلام، وعند الأحناف: لا تقع الفرقة قبل عرض الإسلام وإباء الآخر قالوا؛ لأن في اسلام أحدهما اختلاف الدين فيوجب فساد النكاح كالردة، قلنا: هذا فاسد وضعاً؛ لأنه أي: المعلل جعل الإسلام علة لزوال الملك، والإسلام عهد عاصماً للملك والحقوق كما أي: أسلم في دار الحرب فقد عصم نفسه وماله وولده الصغير، فلا يكون مؤثراً في زوال الملك كذا في "الفصول". فلا يكون مؤثراً إلخ: بل المؤثر في الحقيقة في زوال الملك هو إباء الآخر عن الإسلام بعد العرض عليه، فإنه علة صالحة للتفريق بينهما كما أنها علة مناسبة للحكم [الشافي: ص ۲۸۲]
The fourth research on qiyas. Chapter on objections directed against qiyas. As for al-‘aks, they mean by it that the questioner adheres to the mu‘allil's original (asl) in a way that forces the mu‘allil to find a point of distinction between the original and the branch. Example: Ornaments are prepared for daily wear, so zakat is not obligatory on them, like everyday clothes. We said: If ornaments were like clothes, then zakat would not be obligatory on men's ornaments like everyday clothes. As for fasād al-waḍ‘, it means that an attribute is made the ‘illa which is not suitable for that ruling. Example: In their statement about the Islam of one spouse: difference of religion has occurred in marriage and thus nullifies it, (this is) fasād al-waḍ‘. The Shafi‘is said: like the apostasy of one spouse, for he made Islam a cause for the removal of marital authority. We said: Islam has been known as protective of authority, so it cannot be effective in removing authority. And likewise in the issue of a free woman’s (financial) means: that he is free and capable of marriage, so it is not permissible for him to marry a slave woman just as if he had a free wife. As for al-‘aks, linguistically it is to return a thing to its first state, and terminologically it is that the questioner adheres to the mu‘allil's original (asl), i.e., what the mu‘allil made the original of analogy, against the opposite of what the mu‘allil made the branch, i.e., the opposite of the ruling that the mu‘allil made the branch, which is the claimed ruling by analogy. Like everyday clothes: so the mu‘allil, for the validity of his analogy, is compelled to accept a difference between the original and the branch, i.e., between men's ornaments and everyday clothes, by saying: men's ornaments are prohibited to use, so daily wear is not realized in them unlike clothes. In the Islam of one (spouse) etc.: they said about the Islam of one of them that separation occurs immediately upon Islam, and according to the Hanafis: separation does not occur before presenting Islam and the other's refusal. They said: because in the Islam of one spouse there is difference of religion, which necessitates nullification of marriage like apostasy. We said: this is corrupt in premise (fasād al-waḍ‘); because the mu‘allil made Islam a cause for the removal of authority, while Islam has been known as protective of authority and rights, just as if one converts to Islam in enemy territory, he has protected himself, his property, and his young child, so it cannot be effective in removing authority. As in al-Fusūl. 'So it cannot be effective etc.': rather, the actual effective factor in removing authority is the other's refusal to accept Islam after it being presented to him, for it is an appropriate cause for separation between them, as it is a suitable cause for the ruling. [Al-Shāfī: p. 282]
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a chapter from al-Shāshī's Uṣūl enumerating and explaining the four standard objections (as‘ilah) used against an analogical argument (qiyās) in Hanafī uṣūl al-fiqh. The types are: al-‘aks (the inverse), fasād al-waḍ‘ (corruption of the premise), al-naqḍ (contradiction), and al-mu‘āraḍah (counterargument). The text defines each and provides examples. Al-‘aks forces the person making the analogy to admit a distinction between the original and the branch, invalidating the analogy. Fasād al-waḍ‘ points out that the attribute chosen as the effective cause (ʿilla) is inherently unsuitable for the ruling. The example given is the Shāfi‘ī argument that the Islam of one spouse invalidates marriage because it introduces a difference of religion, analogous to apostasy. The Hanafī respond that Islam is protective of rights, not destructive, so it cannot be the cause for removal of marital authority; rather, the true cause is the other spouse's refusal after presentation. This illustrates how a mis-selected ʿilla can be challenged. The third and fourth objections (al-naqḍ and al-mu‘āraḍah) are briefly mentioned but elaborated in the next lesson. This passage is foundational for understanding how Hanafī scholars critiqued and defended analogical reasoning.

Key Terms
العكس (al-‘aks): An objection in which the questioner adheres to the proponent's original (asl) to force the proponent to admit a distinction between original and branch.
فساد الوضع (fasād al-waḍ‘): An objection that the attribute designated as the illa is inherently unsuitable for the ruling.
النقض (al-naqḍ): An objection that the claimed illa exists but the ruling is absent, either due to a preventer or not.
المعارضة (al-mu‘āraḍah): An objection in which the questioner brings an alternative proof that negates the ruling of the proponent's proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the objection of naqḍ (contradiction) involve?
A) Forcing the proponent to find a distinction between original and branch.
B) Asserting that the chosen illa is unsuitable for the ruling.
C) Showing that the claimed illa exists but the ruling is absent.
D) Bringing an alternative proof that negates the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The four objections to qiyas—‘aks, fasād al-waḍ‘, naqḍ, and mu‘āraḍah—are essential tools for testing the validity of analogical reasoning in Hanafi usul.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
